Oath Concerning a Slave or House After Sale — The Principle of Nisbah (Relation)
Ruling on an oath tied to property ownership after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an oath's subject changes ownership and apply the principle of nisbah (relation) to determine if the oath is broken.

Core Idea
An oath tied to ownership lapses when ownership ends — the relation, not the essence, is the target.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are interpreted according to the intention of the swearer, but when the object of the oath has a specific relation (such as ownership) attached to it, the ruling depends on whether that relation is an essential condition. This lesson examines a classic case: swearing not to speak to 'Zayd's slave' only applies as long as Zayd owns the slave. Once Zayd sells him, the relation ends, and the oath is not broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its subject
Read the oath: 'I will not speak to Zayd's slave' or 'I will not enter Zayd's house'. Note the possessive construction (idafah) linking the object to a specific owner.
The possessive indicates a relation, not just the object itself.
2
Determine the legal cause of the prohibition
The prohibition is tied to the ownership relation. The swearer did not intend to forbid the act absolutely, only under the condition of that ownership.
The underlying principle is that oaths follow the intention (al-umur bi-maqasidiha).
3
Recognize the change in status
Zayd sells the slave or house. The ownership relation is severed. The object no longer belongs to Zayd.
Sale is a clear example; similar transfers (gift, bequest) would have the same effect.
4
Apply the principle: nisbah vs dhat
The oath targeted the relation (nisbah), not the essence (dhat) of the slave or house. Once the relation is gone, the subject of the oath no longer exists in the intended sense.
This is a key Hanafi maxim: when a noun is in idafah, it denotes the relation, not the absolute.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, speaking to the slave or entering the house after the sale does not break the oath. No sin, no kaffarah.
The oath remains valid but inapplicable unless the relation returns (e.g., repurchase).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حلف ان لا يكلم عبد فلان او لايدخل دار فلان فباع فلان عبده او داره ثـم كـلـم الـعبد ودخل الدار لم يحنث [٢٦٨٤] (۴۱) [Urdu: کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ فلاں کے غلام سے بات نہیں کرے گا یا فلاں کے گھر میں داخل نہیں ہوگا، پس فلاں نے غلام نبیچ دیا یا اپنا گھر بیچ دیا پھر غلام سے بات کی یا گھر میں داخل ہوا تو حانث نہیں ہو گا۔ یہاں فلاں کے غلام یا فلاں کے گھر سے نام مقصود نہیں ہے اور نہ اس کی ذات مقصود ہے بلکہ فلاں کے غلام ہوتے ہوئے یا فلاں کا گھر ہوتے ہوئے بات نہیں کرے گا، پس جب غلام کو بیچ دیا یا مکان کو بیچ دیا تو اس کا غلام یا اس کا مکان نہیں رہا۔ اس لئے اس سے بات کرنے یا اس گھر میں داخل ہونے سے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔
اصول ذات مراد نہیں ہے بلکہ اس آدمی کی نسبت مراد ہے۔]
And whoever swore that he will not speak to the slave of so-and-so or not enter the house of so-and-so, then so-and-so sold his slave or his house, then he spoke to the slave or entered the house, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
Here, the oath is about the slave/house in relation to a specific owner. Once the owner sells them, the attribute of belonging to that person is gone. Therefore, speaking or entering no longer violates the oath. The Urdu commentary adds a principle: It is not the essence (dhat) that is intended, but the relation (nisbat) to the named person.

Key Terms
نسبة (nisbah): Relation or attribution; in this context, the connection to the owner

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to enter 'Khalid's house', and Khalid sells the house to another, the swearer enters after the sale. What is the ruling?
A) Oath broken
B) Oath not broken
C) Oath becomes invalid
D) Need to renew oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath attached to a relation (like ownership) only binds while the relation exists; its removal nullifies the oath's applicability.

Oath to Pay Debt: Payment with Defective Coins
Ruling on oath fulfillment with defective coins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether paying a debt with counterfeit or defective coins breaks an oath, and explain the underlying principle of essence (dhat) vs. name.

Core Idea
Paying defective coins fulfills an oath as long as the coin's essence remains a dirham.

When a person swears to pay a debt by a certain time and pays, but the creditor later discovers the coins are counterfeit, defective, or legally claimed by another, does the oath still hold? The Hanafi school rules based on the substance of the coin, not its quality. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is swearing an oath to pay a debt today and actually paying it. The ruling is that the oath is not broken even if the coins are defective.
The matn says 'lam yahnath al-ḥalif' – the swearer does not break his oath.
2
Distinguish the three defective coin types
Understand the three categories of defective coins: zuyuf (counterfeit that even the treasury rejects), nubharjah (defective coins that merchants reject but the treasury accepts), and mustahaqqah (coins belonging to another person). All three share the common feature of being defective yet still dirhams in substance.
3
Understand the legal principle
The commentary states the rule: if the thing paid is essentially the same as what was sworn (i.e., a dirham in reality), even with a defect, the oath is fulfilled. If it is a different substance (e.g., lead), the oath is broken.
The key is essence (dhat) not name (ism).
4
Apply the principle to the three scenarios
In all three scenarios, the coins, though defective, are still legally dirhams. Therefore, the oath is not broken. Only if the paid item is not a dirham at all would the oath be broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲۷۲۵ (۸۲) ومن حلف ليقضين فلانا دينه اليوم فقضاه ثم وجد فلان بعضه زيوفا او نبهرجة او مستحقة لم يحنث الحالف
تشریح یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جس چیز کی قسم کھائی اسی فیصد وہی چیز ہے البتہ کچھ خامی ہے تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اگر اسی فیصد دوسری چیز ہے یعنی مثلا وہ نام کے اعتبار سے درہم ہے ورنہ حقیقت میں وہ درہم نہیں ہے تو اس کو ادا کرنے سے حانث ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ وہی چیز ادا نہیں کی جس کی قسم کھائی ہے۔ اب صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ کسی نے قسم کھائی کہ آج فلاں کو ضرور قرض ادا کروں گا، پس قسم کے مطابق آج ہی قرض ادا کر دیا لیکن ان دراہم میں کھوٹ پن تھا جو تھے تو دراہم لیکن بیت المال نہیں لیتا تھا یا تا ج نہیں لیتا تھا جس کو نہرجہ در ہم کہتے ہیں۔ یا اس درہم میں کسی کا حق نکل گیا تو حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ چاہے کھوٹا سکہ ادا کیا ہو لیکن کیا تو ہے درہم ہی اس لئے حانث نہیں ہوگا۔
[2725 (82)] Whoever swears an oath to pay a debt to so-and-so today, then he pays it, then so-and-so finds some of it to be counterfeit (zuyuf) or nubharjah (defective non-circulating) or subject to another's right (mustahaqqah), the swearer does not break his oath. Commentary: This issue is based on the principle that if the thing for which the oath was taken is essentially the same thing, albeit with some defect, then the oath is not broken. But if it is a different thing in essence, meaning it is called a dirham in name but in reality it is not a dirham, then by paying it, the oath is broken because he did not pay the thing he swore. In this case, he swore to pay the debt today, paid today, but the dirhams had some debasement; they were dirhams but the treasury (bayt al-mal) would not accept them, or merchants would not accept them (nubharjah), or the dirham was subject to another's right; then he does not break his oath because even though he paid a counterfeit coin, it is still a dirham, so he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This ruling deals with oaths to pay a debt using specific coins. The matn says that if a person swears to pay a debt today and pays it, but the creditor later finds the coins to be counterfeit (zuyuf, which even the treasury rejects), nubharjah (defective coins that merchants reject but the treasury accepts), or mustahaqqah (coins that belong to someone else), the swearer does not break his oath (lam yahnath). The commentary explains the key principle: the oath is about the substance of the dirham, not its quality. As long as the coins are still legally considered dirhams (i.e., silver metal with official sanction), even if defective, the oath is fulfilled. Only if the coin is something else entirely (e.g., lead) would the oath be broken.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit or debased coins that even the treasury (bayt al-mal) does not accept.
نبهرجة (nubharjah): Defective coins that merchants refuse but the treasury accepts.
مستحقة (mustahaqqah): Coins that actually belong to another person or are subject to a rightful claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears to pay a debt today and pays with counterfeit coins that even the treasury rejects (zuyuf), according to the Hanafi ruling, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the coins are not acceptable currency.
B) No, because the coins are still considered dirhams in essence.
C) Yes, unless the creditor accepts them.
D) No, only if he pays in full weight.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school focuses on the substance (dhat) of the coin: as long as the paid item is fundamentally a dirham, even if defective, the oath is not broken.

Competing Claims to a Purchased Slave: The Hanafi Option (Khiyar) Solution
Explaining that each claimant has the option to take half the slave or leave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two claimants each present equal evidence of purchase of the same slave, and apply the option (khiyar) to each claimant.

Core Idea
When two buyers present equal evidence, each may take half the slave for half the price or walk away—they cannot be forced to share.

Imagine two people each claiming to have bought the same slave from the same seller, with equally strong evidence on the same date. How does a Hanafi judge resolve this? In this lesson, we explore the ruling where each claimant is given the option to take half the slave for half the price or reject the claim entirely. This prevents injustice and respects both claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Two claimants, same slave, same seller, equal evidence. No preference in date or witness quality.
2
Understand the default
The judge cannot award the whole slave to one party because evidence is equal. There is no basis for preference.
In Hanafi law, equal evidence leads to equal shares.
3
Recognize the solution
The judge deems each claimant to have bought half the slave, but each has the option (khiyar) to accept or reject.
This avoids forced co-ownership.
4
Know the basis
The ruling is derived from the hadith of Abu Musa where the Prophet divided a camel equally between two claimants with equal evidence.
The hadith is applied analogically to a purchase claim.
5
Note the optional nature
The option is individual: each decides independently. If both accept, they become co-owners; if one rejects, the other gets the whole slave? (Not explicitly stated, but the text implies each has the choice.)
The exact outcome if one rejects is not stated; but the principle is that the claimant is not forced to accept half.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ادعى اثنان كل واحد منهما انه اشترى منه هذا العبد و اقاما البينة فكل واحد منهما بالخيار ان شاء اخذ نصف العبد بنصف الثمن وان شاء ترک [۲۷۴۸] (۱۹) وإن ادعى اثنان كل واحد منهما أنه اشترى هذا العبد من فلان وأقاما البينة، فكل واحد منهما بالخيار: إن شاء أخذ نصف العبد بنصف الثمن، وإن شاء ترك. مثاله: زيد وعمر كل يدعي أنه اشترى العبد خالدا من البائع ساجد، وأقاما البينة وتاريخهما واحد. فلا مرجح، فيقضى بأن كل واحد اشترى نصف العبد، ويلزمه نصف الثمن. ولكل واحد الخيار في الأخذ والترك. والدليل حديث أبي موسى في البعير.
And if two people each claim that he bought this slave from him, and they present evidence, then each has the option: if he wishes, he may take half the slave for half the price, and if he wishes, he may leave it. [2748] (19) If two people each claim that he bought this slave from so-and-so and present evidence, then each has the option: if he wishes, he takes half the slave for half the price, and if he wishes, he leaves it. Example: Zayd and 'Umar each claim to have bought the slave Khalid from the seller Sajid. They present evidence and their dates are the same. So there is no preference, so it is judged that each bought half the slave, and each owes half the price. Each has the option to take or leave. The proof is the hadith of Abu Musa about the camel.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This ruling applies to a case where two people claim to have purchased the same slave from the same seller, with equal evidence. Since the slave cannot be fully owned by both, the judge gives each the option to take half for half the price or to abandon the claim. This is based on the hadith of the camel, where the Prophet divided the item equally. However, in a purchase contract, the price is also split. The option (khiyar) allows the claimants to avoid a forced partnership if they do not wish to share. This ruling assumes no preference in time or witnesses. The example illustrates: Zayd and Umar both present evidence of purchase from the same seller, with identical dates. Since no preference exists, each is deemed to have bought half, and each may either take that half by paying half the price or reject it.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice given to each claimant to accept or reject the half.
بيّنة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of two claimants each claiming to have bought the same slave from the same seller with equal evidence and no date precedence, what option does each have?
A) Each can take the whole slave by paying the full price.
B) Each can take half the slave for half the price or leave it.
C) Each must take half the slave and pay half the price.
D) Each can force the other to accept a buyout.

Key Takeaway
When two claimants present equal evidence of purchase of the same item, the Hanafi judge grants each the option to take half for half the price or reject the claim, preventing forced co-ownership.

Oath Wording in Sale Disputes: Preventing Tawriyah
Ruling on oath wording to prevent tawriyah in sale disputes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the proper oath wording when a seller denies a sale and explain how the wording blocks mental reservation (tawriyah).

Core Idea
Focus the oath on the ongoing sale, not the past act, to block mental reservation.

How should a judge word the oath when a seller denies a claimed sale? A careless wording could allow the defendant to swear truthfully without admitting the debt. Imam Abu Hanifa specifies a precise phrasing that eliminates loopholes. This lesson examines the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer claims to have purchased a slave for 1000 dirhams; the seller denies. The judge must administer an oath to the seller.
Standard procedure for denial of a contract.
2
Contrast the two oath wordings
One wording: 'By Allah, I did not sell' (ما بعت). Another: 'By Allah, there is no ongoing sale between you two' (ما بينكما بيع قائم).
The second is what Abu Hanifa orders; the first is to be avoided.
3
Analyze why 'I did not sell' is problematic
If the seller had previously made a sale but then rescinded it, he can truthfully say 'I did not sell' meaning 'I did not sell now' — a tawriyah.
Tawriyah: mental reservation that makes the oath technically true but deceptive.
4
Explain why 'no ongoing sale' blocks evasion
The seller cannot use tawriyah because the oath focuses on the present existence of a sale. If the contract was rescinded, there is indeed no ongoing sale, so the oath is straightforward.
This wording forces the seller to either admit the sale or perjure himself, preventing deception.
5
Note the authority and principle
This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa (ra) and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling in the Hanafi school. The general principle: in transactions that may be rescinded, swear on the current existence of the transaction, not on the act of sale.
The juristic reason (talil) is blocking the means to fraud (sadd al-dhara'i').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[2775] (46) ومن ادعى انه ابتاع من هذا عبده بالف فجحده استحلف بالله ما بينكما بيع قائم فيه ولا يستحلف بالله بالله ما بعت [2775] (46) کسی نے دعوی کیا کہ خریدا ہے اس سے اس کے غلام کو ہزار کے بدلے میں ، پس اس نے انکار کیا تو قسم کھلا ئیں گے کہ خدا کی قسم تم دونوں کے درمیان ابھی بیچ قائم نہیں ہے۔ اور یوں قسم نہیں کھلائیں گے کہ خدا کی قسم میں نے نہیں بیچا ہے۔ امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک قسم کھلانے کا یہ امتیاضی پہلو ہے نا کہ یہ ہی چکما دے کر نکل نہ جائے اور توریہ کر کے قسم نہ کھالے۔ اس لئے یوں قسم دے گا کہ خدا کی قسم ابھی تم دونوں کے درمیان بیع قائم نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ پہلے بچا ہولیکن بیع تو ڑ دی ہو تو یہ کہہ سکتا ہے کہ میں نے ابھی تو نہیں بیچا میں نے تو بہت پہلے پہچا تھا اس لئے قسم کھا سکتا ہے کہ ابھی نہیں بیچا۔ اور یوں قسم کھلا ئیں کہ ابھی بھی بیع قائم ہے تو تو یہ نہیں کر سکا اور توریہ کر کے قسم نہیں کھا سکتا۔ اس لئے وہ معاملات جو بعد میں ٹوٹ سکتے ہوں حاصل کلام پر قسم کھلائیں گے۔ معاملات کرنے پر قسم نہیں کھلا ئیں گے۔
(46) And whoever claims that he bought from this person his slave for a thousand, and he (the seller) denies it, he (the seller) is made to swear: 'By Allah, there is no ongoing sale between you two.' And he is not made to swear: 'By Allah, I did not sell.' (46) Someone claimed that he bought the slave from him for a thousand, then he denied, so he will be made to swear: 'By Allah, there is no sale existing between you now.' And they will not make him swear: 'By Allah, I did not sell.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this is a distinguishing aspect of administering the oath so that he does not deceive and does not take an oath with tawriyah (mental reservation). Therefore, he will swear: 'By Allah, there is no sale between you now.' Because it is possible that he had sold earlier but then rescinded the sale, so he could say, 'I did not sell now; I sold a long time ago.' So he could take the oath that he did not sell now. And if they make him swear that there is no sale now, he cannot do that and cannot use tawriyah. Therefore, in transactions that may be later rescinded, they will be sworn on the outcome (the current existence), not on the act of selling.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where the plaintiff claims to have bought a slave from the defendant for a thousand dirhams, and the defendant denies. The judge must administer the oath carefully to prevent the defendant from using tawriyah (mental reservation) to swear falsely while technically telling the truth. The Hanafi ruling, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, is to make the defendant swear: 'By Allah, there is no ongoing sale between you two' (ما بينكما بيع قائم) rather than 'By Allah, I did not sell' (بالله ما بعت). The reason is that if the defendant had made a sale earlier but then rescinded it, he could truthfully say 'I did not sell' meaning 'I did not sell now' while the sale may have existed in the past. By focusing on the present existence of the sale (بيع قائم), the defendant cannot use this evasion. This shows the Hanafi school's precision in oath wording to ensure justice.

Key Terms
تورية (tawriyah): Mental reservation or equivocation in an oath to avoid perjury while technically telling the truth.
بيع قائم (bay' qā'im): An existing/ongoing sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what wording should a judge use when administering an oath to a seller who denies a sale?
A) 'By Allah, I did not sell.'
B) 'By Allah, there is no ongoing sale between you two.'
C) 'By Allah, I never owned this item.'
D) 'By Allah, this transaction never happened.'

Key Takeaway
In sale denial oaths, always focus on the present existence of the contract to prevent tawriyah.

Evidence in Seller-Buyer Disputes Over Price or Quantity
Hanafi rules on deciding conflicts between buyer and seller when only one brings evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that when only one party brings evidence in a sale dispute, judgment is given for that party, and when both bring evidence, the evidence of the party claiming extra is preferred.

Core Idea
When both have evidence, the one claiming extra wins—he is the true claimant for the surplus.

This lesson deals with a common scenario in sales: buyer and seller disagree on the price or quantity. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule derived from the Prophet's hadith: evidence is on the claimant. The passage explains how to determine who is the claimant when both parties could be considered such, and what happens when both bring evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Determine what the buyer and seller disagree on: price or quantity. Understand that each party can be a claimant from one angle and defendant from another.
2
Apply the hadith principle
Recall the hadith: 'البينة على المدعى واليمين على المدعى عليه' (Evidence is on the claimant, oath on the defendant). This is the foundation.
3
Rule when only one has evidence
If only the buyer brings evidence, he wins; if only the seller brings evidence, he wins. The one with evidence is deemed the claimant for his claim.
4
Rule when both have evidence
Both bring evidence; now whose evidence is preferred? The Hanafi rule: the evidence of the one claiming extra (zayadati) is preferred. He is the claimant regarding the extra.
5
Rationale and cross-madhhab note
The one claiming extra is asserting a greater right, so his evidence is more specific. Some other schools may treat both equally.

Source Text

[۲۷۸۴ (۵۵) واذا اختلف المتبايعان في البيع فادعى المشترى ثمنا وادعى البائع اكثر منه او اعترف البائع بقدر من المبيع وادعى المشترى اكثر منه واقام احدهما البينة قضى له بها [ ۲۷۸۵] (۵۶) فان اقام كل واحد المدعی علیہ ص ۲۴۹ نمبر ۱۳۴۱) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ مدعی پر گواہ اور مدعی علیہ پر قسم ہے۔ [۲۷۸۵] (۵۶) پس اگر دونوں میں سے ہر ایک نے بینہ قائم کیا تو زیادتی ثابت کرنے والے کا بینہ زیادہ اولی ہوگا۔ چونکہ ان مسئلوں میں دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ ہیں اس لئے دونوں بینہ قائم کر سکتے ہیں۔ اب دونوں نے بینہ قائم کر دیا تو کس کے بعینہ کو ترجیح دیں گے۔ تو فرماتے ہیں کہ جس کا بینہ زیادتی کو ثابت کرتا ہو وہ اصل میں زیادتی کا مدعی ہے اور دوسرا اس کا انکار کرتا ہے اس حاشیہ : (الف) آپ نے خطبے میں فرمایا گواہ دگی پر ہے اور قسم مدعی علیہ پر ہے۔
[2784] (55) When the buyer and seller dispute in a sale, and the buyer claims a price and the seller claims more, or the seller admits a quantity of goods and the buyer claims more, and one of them brings evidence, judgment is given according to that evidence. [2785] (56) If each of them brings evidence... (55) The seller and buyer disputed in a sale. Then the buyer claimed one price and the seller claimed more, or the seller admitted one quantity of the goods and the buyer claimed more. And if one of them establishes evidence, judgment is given for him. These two issues are based on the principle that here both seller and buyer can be claimants and defendants. Since both are claimants, whoever establishes evidence will be decided according to his evidence, and the other will be considered defendant. For example, the buyer claims he bought this goat for ten dirhams, so give me the goat. Then the buyer becomes claimant, the seller defendant. In this case, the buyer's evidence should be accepted, and the seller must take an oath. But if the seller claims more, e.g., twelve dirhams, saying give me twelve dirhams and take the goat, then the seller becomes claimant, buyer defendant, so the seller's evidence should be accepted. So whoever presents evidence, his testimony is accepted and judgment is given in his favor. In the second issue, the seller claims a specific quantity: e.g., the seller says I sold ten kilos of wheat for ten dirhams, so give ten dirhams and take ten kilos. From that aspect, seller is claimant, buyer defendant, so seller's evidence is considered. And the buyer claims more goods: e.g., says the deal was for twelve kilos for ten dirhams. Then buyer is claimant, seller defendant, so buyer's evidence should be accepted. In both issues, both can be claimant and defendant, so whoever establishes evidence, it is accepted and judgment given. The hadith has passed: from Amr bin Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet ﷺ said in his sermon: Evidence is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant. (Tirmidhi) In this hadith, evidence is on the claimant, oath on the defendant. [2785] (56) If each of them brings evidence, then the evidence of the one proving extra is more entitled. Because in these issues both are claimants and defendants, so both can bring evidence. Now if both bring evidence, whose evidence is preferred? He says: The one whose evidence proves extra; in reality he is the claimant of extra, and the other denies it. Footnote: He said in the sermon: evidence is on the claimant, oath on the defendant.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage addresses disputes between buyer and seller in a sale contract where they disagree on the price or the quantity of goods. The commentary explains that in such cases, both parties can be considered claimants (mudda'i) from one perspective and defendants (mudda'a 'alayh) from another. The guiding rule is the Prophetic hadith: 'Evidence is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant.' Therefore, if only one party brings evidence (baiyina), that evidence is accepted and judgment is given accordingly. For example, if the buyer claims the price was 10 dirhams and the seller claims it was 12, then if the buyer brings witnesses, he wins; if the seller brings witnesses, he wins. The same applies when they dispute over the quantity of goods. When both parties present evidence, the ruling [2785] states that the evidence of the one claiming the greater amount (zayadati) is preferred. The rationale is that the one claiming extra is actually making a claim that requires proof, while the other is merely denying it. This is a refinement of the general principle: when both have evidence, the evidence of the party asserting a greater right takes precedence because it is stronger in establishing a surplus. The commentary emphasizes that the hadith from Tirmidhi provides the foundation for these rules.

Key Terms
بینہ (baiyina): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females for financial claims.
مدعی (mudda'i): The claimant who must provide evidence; here both parties can be mudda'i from different angles.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant; here the party against whom the claim is directed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over the price of a goat, the buyer claims 10 dirhams and the seller claims 15 dirhams. Both bring witnesses. According to Hanafi fiqh, whose evidence is preferred?
A) The buyer's evidence
B) The seller's evidence
C) Both are rejected because they conflict
D) The judge decides by lot

Key Takeaway
When both parties bring evidence in a sale dispute, the Hanafi school prefers the evidence of the party claiming extra because that party is the true claimant for the surplus.

Preference of Evidence Proving Excess in Sale Disputes
Ruling on preferring evidence establishing excess

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the evidence proving an excess (ziyada) is preferred in sale disputes, and identify which party's evidence is accepted in given scenarios.

Core Idea
In conflicting sale claims, the evidence proving the excess is preferred, as the burden of proof lies with the claimant of the extra.

This lesson introduces a fundamental Hanafi principle in sale disputes when both parties have evidence. The rule is simple: the evidence that establishes an excess (ziyada) is given priority. The Urdu commentary illustrates two scenarios: if the seller claims a higher price, his evidence prevails; if the buyer claims more goods, his evidence prevails. This principle reflects the burden of proof on the one asserting something additional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Determine what each party claims (price or quantity) and who is asserting an excess over the other party's admission.
The excess is the difference between the two claims.
2
Apply the preference rule
The evidence of the party claiming the excess is preferred. Their witnesses are accepted.
It does not matter whether the party is the seller or buyer; the only criterion is who claims more.
3
Determine the outcome
The judge accepts the evidence of the excess claimant, thereby ruling in their favour on that particular issue.
If multiple issues exist (e.g., price and payment), apply the rule independently.
4
Note the cross‑madhhab status
This is the Hanafi position. Other schools may not give blanket preference and may require additional scrutiny.
Within the Hanafi school, this ruling is from Zahir al‑Riwayah and is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهما البينة كانت البيئة المثبتة للزيادة اولى [۲۷۸۶] (۵) مثلا پہلے مسئلے میں بائع زیادہ ثمن کا دعوی کرتا ہے اس لئے اس کے گواہ مقبول ہوں گے۔ اور دوسرے مسئلے میں مشتری زیادہ بیع کا دعوی کرتا ہے اس لئے اس کے گواہ مقبول ہوں گے۔
Of the two, the evidence that proves the excess is preferred. For example, in the first case, the seller claims more price, so his witnesses are accepted. And in the second case, the buyer claims more of the sold goods, so his witnesses are accepted.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school regarding sale disputes when both parties present evidence. The text states that if both parties have evidence, the evidence that establishes an excess (ziyada) is given priority. The Urdu commentary clarifies two scenarios: first, if the seller claims a higher price than the buyer admits, the seller's witnesses are accepted; second, if the buyer claims more goods than the seller admits, the buyer's witnesses are accepted. This principle is based on the idea that the excess is what is disputed, and the burden of proof lies on the one asserting it.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, as per Hanafi law.
زيادة (ziyada): The excess or additional amount claimed by one party over what the other admits.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sale dispute, if both the seller and buyer present evidence, and the seller claims a higher price while the buyer claims a lower price, which evidence is preferred according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer's evidence, because the buyer is the defendant.
B) The seller's evidence, because the seller is claiming an excess.
C) Neither, the judge calls for additional witnesses.
D) The judge decides based on the credibility of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when both parties present evidence in a sale dispute, the evidence proving an excess (ziyada) is preferred.

Procedure When Neither Party Has Evidence in Sale Disputes
Procedure: offer to accept, then oaths, inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi judicial procedure for sale disputes without evidence and explain why the buyer swears first, contrasting with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
When evidence is absent, the judge offers compromise, then oaths; the buyer swears first as defendant in price.

In a sale dispute where neither party has witnesses, the Hanafi school prescribes a step-by-step judicial procedure. The judge first encourages settlement, then administers oaths. A key difference with the Shafi'i school arises over who swears first. This lesson explores both the procedure and the legal reasoning behind the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Initial Offer
Recognize the condition: no evidence from either party. The judge sequentially offers each party a chance to accept the other's claim, warning that refusal leads to rescission.
This is a settlement attempt before resorting to oaths.
2
Explain the Rationale for Settlement Offers
The judge offers the buyer to accept the seller's price, and the seller to accept the buyer's claimed goods. This encourages compromise and preserves the contract.
Based on the principle of preserving contracts when possible.
3
Describe the Oath Procedure if No Settlement
If both refuse, the judge administers oaths to both parties on each other's claims, starting with the buyer. If both swear, the judge rescinds the sale.
The buyer swears first because he is the defendant regarding the price.
4
Analyze the Disputed Point: Who Swears First
The Hanafi school holds the buyer swears first, applying the general principle that the defendant (munkir) swears first. The Shafi'i school argues that lots should determine the order.
This is the core ikhtilaf in this passage.
5
Evaluate the Shafi'i Objection and Hanafi Response
The Shafi'i rely on the hadith 'استهما على اليمين' (cast lots for the oath). The Hanafi response interprets 'lots' as emphasizing that both must swear, not the order; the order is derived by qiyas.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text

فان لم يكن لكل واحد منهما بينة قيل للمشترى اما ان ترضى بالثمن الذى ادعاه البائع والا فسخنا البيع وقيل للبائع اما ان تسلّم ما ادعاه المشترى من المبيع والا فسخنا البيع [۲۷] (۵۸) فان لم يتراضيا استخلف الحاكم كل واحد منهما على دعوى الآخر ويبتدئ بيمين المشترى فاذا حلفا فسخ القاضي البيع بينهما 
پس اگر دونوں میں سے کسی کے لئے بینہ نہ ہو تو مشتری سے کہا جائے گا کہ یا راضی ہو جائے اس قیمت پر جس کا بائع دعوی کرتا ہے ورنہ تو ہم بیع فسخ کر دیں گے۔ اور بائع سے کہا جائے گا یا مان لے اس بات کو جس کا مشتری دعوی کرتا ہے مبیع میں سے ورنہ تو ہم بیع فخ کر دیں گے۔
اگر دونوں میں سے کسی کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے تو حاکم پہلے مسئلے میں مشتری سے کہے گا کہ یا تو بائع جتنا کہتا ہے مثلا بارہ درہم کا دعوی ہے تو بارہ در ہم کو مان لو اور بکری لے لودر نہ بیچ فسخ کر دیں گے۔ یہ اس لئے کہے گا تا کہ ہوسکتا ہے کہ شیخ کے ڈر سے مشتری مان لے اور بیچ باقی رکھے۔ اس طرح دوسرے مسئلے میں بائع سے کہے گا کہ یا تو مشتری جتنے مبیع کا دعوی کرتا ہے مثلا دس درہم میں بار کیلوگیہوں کا تو اس کو مان لواور بیچ باقی رکھوور نہ تو بیع فسخ کر دوں گا۔ یہ اس لئے کہ ممکن ہے کہ بیچ ٹوٹنے کے خوف سے بائع مشتری کی بات مان لے۔
تسلم : بات مان لے تسلیم کرلے۔ (۲۷۸۷] (۵۸) پس اگر دونوں راضی نہ ہوں تو حاکم دونوں میں سے ہر ایک سے قسم لے دوسرے کے دعوی پر اور شروع کرے مشتری کی قسم سے، پس اگر دونوں قسم کھا لیں تو قاضی ان کی بیچ کو فتح کر دے۔
چونکہ دونوں مدعی علیہ بھی ہیں اور مدعی کے پاس بینہ نہیں ہے اس لئے دونوں کو دوسرے کے دعوی پر قسم کھلائیں گے۔ اور چونکہ دونوں کو قتسم کھلایا اور کوئی ترجیح کی چیز نہیں ہے اور نہ یہ کر سکتے ہیں کہ دونوں کے درمیان آدھے آدھے کا فیصلہ کر دیں۔ اس لئے آخری صورت یہ ہے کہ بیچ کو فتح کر دیا جائے (۲) حدیث میں ہے کہ دونوں کے پاس بینہ نہ ہو تو دونوں قسم کھا ئیں۔ عن ابی هریرة ان رجلين اختصما في متاع الى النبي عل الله ليس لواحد منهما بينة فقال النبي عل الله استهما على اليمين ما كان احبا ذلک او کرها (الف)(ابو

حاشیہ : (الف) ایک سامان کے بارے میں دو آدمی حضور کے سامنے جھگڑا لے گئے ۔ ان میں سے کسی کے لئے گواہ نہیں تھا تو حضور نے فرمایا تم دونوں قسم پر قرعہ ڈالو ( اور دونوں قسمیں کھاؤ) چاہے اس کو پسند کرو چاہے پسند نہ کرو۔
فسخ القاضي البيع بينهما 
امام شافعی کی رائے ہے کہ کون پہلے قسم کھائے اس کے لئے قرعہ ڈالے۔ جس کا نام قرعہ میں نکلے وہ پہلے قسم کھائے۔ اوپر والی حدیث استھما علی الیمین سے بھی اس طرف اشارہ ہے کہ قسم کے بارے میں قرعہ ڈالے اسلئے پہلے تم کھانے کے لئے قرعہ ڈالا جائے گا۔
If neither of them has evidence, it is said to the buyer: either accept the price claimed by the seller, or else we rescind the sale. And it is said to the seller: either surrender what the buyer claims of the sold goods, or else we rescind the sale. (58) Then if they do not agree, the judge administers an oath to each of them concerning the other's claim, and begins with the oath of the buyer. If they both swear, the judge rescinds the sale between them. [Urdu commentary: explanation of the procedure, hadith from Abu Hurairah about two men claiming a commodity with no evidence, the Prophet said: 'Cast lots for the oath, whether they like it or dislike it.' Then: The judge rescinds the sale between them. Imam Shafi'i's opinion is that lots should be cast to determine who swears first.]
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an inter-madhhab dispute regarding sale disputes when there is no evidence. The Hanafi position states that if neither party produces evidence, the judge first offers the buyer the option to accept the seller's claimed price; if he refuses, the sale is rescinded. Similarly, the seller is offered to accept the buyer's claimed goods; if he refuses, the sale is rescinded. This is to encourage settlement. If they still do not agree, the judge administers oaths to both, starting with the buyer, because the buyer is the one initially denying payment. If both swear, the sale is rescinded, as there is no way to establish truth. The hadith from Abu Hurairah supports the use of oaths when neither has evidence, but the Prophet mentioned casting lots for the oath (istiham). The Hanafi school interprets this as indicating that both must swear, but the order is determined by legal reasoning, not lots. Imam Shafi'i argues that lots should be used to decide who swears first, citing the same hadith. The Hanafi response is that the buyer swears first because he is the defendant in the claim of price, and the principle is that the defendant swears first. The hadith's reference to lots is about the distribution of the oath, not the order.

Key Terms
استخلف (istakhlafa): To administer an oath to someone, especially in judicial proceedings.
فسخ البيع (faskh al-bay'): Rescission of the sale contract by the judge due to inability to resolve the dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if neither the buyer nor the seller has evidence in a price dispute, who swears first?
A) Neither, the judge decides by lot.
B) The seller swears first.
C) The buyer swears first.
D) Both swear simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
In sale disputes without evidence, the judge first offers settlement, then administers oaths starting with the buyer, and rescinds the sale if both swear.

Legal Effect of Refusal to Swear (Nukool) in Sale Disputes
Ruling: refusal to take oath makes the opponent's claim binding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on nukool and apply it to a sale dispute where one party refuses the oath.

Core Idea
Refusal to swear (nukool) is legally equivalent to a second witness, making the opponent's claim binding.

In disputes over sale transactions, when both parties have no witnesses, the judge offers them the oath. If one party refuses to swear, what happens? This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling that nukool establishes the opponent's claim. The principle is derived from a hadith about divorce and applied analogically to all claims. Understanding this rule clarifies how Hanafi courts resolve evidentiary stalemates.

Guided Path
1
Name the act and its ruling
Identify the act: refusal to take the oath after being asked by the judge (nukool). The ruling is that the opposite party's claim becomes binding (lazim) and the judge decides accordingly.
This applies in sale disputes and other claims.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb, where the Prophet ruled that a husband's refusal to swear in a divorce case is equivalent to a second witness, making the divorce valid.
Hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative phrase 'فنكوله بمنزلة شاهد آخر' directly indicates that refusal functions like a witness, establishing the opponent's claim. The same principle applies by qiyas to all disputes, including sale.
The analogy (qiyas) is based on the common illa: both involve a claim and a refusal to rebut.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require additional evidence or treat nukool as merely a presumption, not an equivalent to a witness. The Hanafi position is that the judge must decide based on the refusal.
The Hanafi view is the mufta bihi.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the apparent meaning of the hadith and the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to apply it broadly. This is the relied-upon position in Zahir al-Riwayah.
No explicit tarjih authority is cited in the passage; it is standard Hanafi doctrine.

فان نكل احدهما عن اليمين لزمه دعوى الآخر [ ۲۷۸۹] (۶۰) 
پس اگر دونوں میں سے ایک قسم سے انکار کرے تو اس پر دوسرے کا دعوی لازم ہوگا۔
بائع اور مشتری کو قسم کھانے کے لئے کہا۔ پس دونوں میں سے ایک نے قسم کھانے سے انکار کر دیا تو اس کے انکار کے دو مطالب ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ میں دبے زبان سے اقرار کرتا ہوں کہ خصم کا دعوی صحیح ہے۔ اور دوسرا مطلب یہ ہے کہ خصم کا دعوی صحیح تو نہیں ہے لیکن چلو اپنی چیز دے دیتا ہوں یہ اللہ کے عظیم نام کے ساتھ قسم کھانے سے بہتر ہے۔ تاہم دونوں صورتوں میں قاضی مد مقابل کے دعوی کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دے گا۔
حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عمر بن شعیب عن ابيه عن جده عن النبي الله قال اذا ادعت المرأة طلاق زوجها فجائت على ذلك بشاهد عدل استحلف زوجها فان حلف بطلت شهادة الشاهد وان نكل فنكوله بمنزلة شاهد آخر وجاز طلاقه (الف) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب الرجل بجد الطلاق ص ۲۹۲ نمبر ۲۰۳۸ ردار قطنی ، کتاب الوکاله ج رابع ص ۶؟ نمبر ۴۲۹۵) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ قسم سے انکار کرنا دوسرے گواہ کے درجے میں ہے۔ اور اس سے مدعی کے دعوی کے مطابق فیصلہ کر دیا جائے گا۔
If one of them refrains from the oath, the claim of the other becomes binding upon him. [Urdu: Explanation that refusal implies either tacit admission or preference to give up property rather than swear; in both cases, the judge decides in favor of the opponent. Evidence: Hadith from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb: if a woman claims divorce from her husband and brings one upright witness, the husband is made to swear; if he swears, the witness's testimony is void; if he refuses, his refusal is equivalent to a second witness and divorce is valid.]
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the legal effect of nukool (refusal to take an oath) in sale disputes. The matn states that if one party refuses the oath, the other's claim becomes binding (lazim). The Urdu commentary elaborates that there are two possible intentions behind the refusal: either tacit admission that the opponent's claim is true, or a preference to give up the disputed property rather than swear by Allah. In both cases, the judge rules against the refuser. The proof is a hadith from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb, where the Prophet ruled that a husband's refusal to swear in a divorce case is equivalent to a second witness, making the divorce valid. This establishes that nukool is treated as evidence against the refuser. This principle applies not only to divorce but to all claims, including sale disputes.

Key Terms
نكل (nakala): Refused to take the oath after being asked by the judge.
نكوله بمنزلة شاهد آخر (nukooluhu bi-manzilati shahidin akhar): His refusal is equivalent to another witness; legal principle from the hadith.

C) The sale is automatically rescinded.
D) The seller is imprisoned until he swears.

Key Takeaway
Refusal to swear (nukool) operates as a legal admission, making the opponent's claim binding and allowing the judge to decide accordingly.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Oaths After Perishing of Sold Item
Presents two Hanafi views on mutual oaths after the sold item perishes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad on oath-taking when the sold item perishes, and state the operative ruling (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
After possession, the buyer becomes the defendant; only his oath is needed, not mutual oaths.

Intra-Hanafi disputes reveal how different principles can generate divergent rulings from the same starting point. Here, the issue is a destroyed item: after the buyer has taken possession, the item perishes, and then the parties disagree on the price. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that because the buyer is now in possession, he is the defendant regarding the price, so only his oath is required. Imam Muhammad, however, treats the price dispute as a conflict about the essence of the contract, leading to mutual oaths and rescission. Understanding this disagreement refines your ability to trace legal reasoning within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute trigger
The sold item (mabī') is destroyed in the buyer's hands after possession, and the parties dispute the agreed price. The question is: how is this dispute resolved procedurally?
The item is gone, so the dispute is purely about the price.
2
State the Shaykhayn's position and its underlying principle
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule: no mutual oaths. Only the buyer takes an oath, and his stated price is accepted. Their principle is that after possession, the buyer is no longer a plaintiff regarding the sale; only the seller is the plaintiff claiming the price, and the buyer is the defendant. So the burden of proof (oath) falls on the defendant.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the school.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position and its underlying principle
Imam Muhammad rules: mutual oaths are taken, the sale is rescinded, and the buyer returns the market value of the perished item. His principle treats the perished item as if it still exists (in value), making both parties simultaneously plaintiffs and defendants regarding the price – thus mutual oaths.
This is a valid alternative within the Hanafi school, though not the relied-upon position.
4
Compare the two principles and identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under the Shaykhayn, the buyer's oath settles the price without rescission; the sale stands with the buyer's stated price. Under Imam Muhammad, the sale is rescinded, and the buyer pays the market value (which may differ from either claimed price).
The difference in principle (possession vs. essence of contract) leads to different outcomes.
5
Apply the operative ruling to a new scenario
Given a similar case (e.g., the item is partially damaged instead of completely destroyed), determine which position would apply and why. Check if the principle of possession still holds.
This step reinforces operational understanding of the mufta bihi.

Source Text

[2790] (61) If the sold item perishes and then the two parties dispute, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, they are not made to take oaths, and the statement of the buyer is accepted regarding the price. And Muhammad said: They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded upon the value of the perished item. ... The principle: this matter is upon the principle that after taking possession of the sold item, only the seller is the plaintiff and only the buyer is the defendant. Muhammad said: They consider the value of the perished item as the original and consider it as existing; in that case both can be plaintiff and both defendant.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific scenario: after the buyer has taken possession of the sold item, it is destroyed in his hands, and then the parties disagree about the price. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Shaykhayn) rule that no mutual oaths are required. Since the buyer has already possessed the item, he is no longer a plaintiff regarding the sale; instead, the seller is now the plaintiff claiming the price, and the buyer is the defendant denying the amount. Therefore, only the buyer's oath is accepted, and he must swear to his stated price. Imam Muhammad differs: he considers the value of the destroyed item as if it still exists, making both parties simultaneously plaintiffs and defendants regarding the price. Thus, mutual oaths are taken, the sale is rescinded, and the buyer must return the market value of the item. The commentary explains the underlying principles: the Shaykhayn focus on the change in possession; Muhammad treats the price dispute as a direct conflict about the essence of the contract.

Key Terms
مبيع (mabī‘): The sold item (goods or property) that is the subject of the sale contract.
بازاری قیمت (bazārī qīmat): Market value of the perished item at the time of rescission; used by Imam Muhammad when the sale is rescinded.
قسم (qasam): Oath; here referring to the legal procedure of taking an oath to resolve an evidentiary standoff.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer's statement is accepted with his oath.
B) Both parties take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded.
C) The seller's statement is accepted with his oath.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
After possession, the buyer becomes the defendant in a price dispute, so only his oath is needed (Shaykhayn, mufta bihi); Imam Muhammad's alternative view requires mutual oaths and rescission.

Dispute on Price After One of Two Slaves Perishes: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mutual oaths after partial loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's rulings on mutual oaths when one of two slaves perishes and identify the underlying legal principle that drives each position.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths on a sale after partial loss depend on whether the dead item's claim is waived or treated as a subsidiary.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a sale of two slaves for a single price, where one slave perishes before a price dispute arises. The central question is: should the parties be made to take mutual oaths (tahaluf) and rescind the sale, or must the seller first forgo the price share of the deceased? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf offer contrasting solutions based on different principles of claim and returnability.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Identify the facts: the seller sold two slaves as a set for one price, the buyer took possession, one slave died, then a dispute arose about the total price.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa rules that mutual oaths are not taken unless the seller voluntarily abandons the share of the deceased slave. Without that, only the buyer takes an oath because he is the defendant regarding the price.
The dead slave cannot be returned, so rescission is impossible for that item.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf rules that mutual oaths are taken immediately on the living slave, and as a subsidiary (tābi‘), also on the value of the deceased. The sale is rescinded for both.
The living slave can be the subject of mutual claim, and the deceased slave's value follows as a dependent.
4
Compare underlying principles
Abu Hanifa focuses on the impossibility of returning the perished slave; Abu Yusuf focuses on the living slave as a present subject that can be the locus of mutual oaths.
This difference affects the procedure: waiver vs. direct mutual oaths.
5
Note the practical difference
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller must give up part of his claim; under Abu Yusuf, the process is simpler and does not require a waiver.
Later Hanafi practice often prefers Abu Yusuf's view for practicality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲۷۹۱] (۶۲) إذا هلک احد العبدين ثم اختلفا في الثمن لم يتحالفا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله إلا أن يرضى البائع أن يترك حصة الهالك. [۲۷۹۲] (۶۳) وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله يتحالفان ويفسخ البيع في الحي وقيمة الهالك. بائع نے دو غلام بیچا تھا، مشتری نے ان پر قبضہ کیا پھر ایک غلام ہلاک ہو گیا۔ اس کے بعد ان کی قیمت میں اختلاف ہوا۔ بائع کہتا ہے کہ دونوں غلام دو ہزار میں بیچا تھا اور مشتری کہتا ہے کہ ایک ہزار میں بیچا تھا تو امام ابو حنیفہ فرماتے ہیں کہ دونوں کو قسمیں نہیں کھلائیں گے بلکہ صرف مشتری کو قسم کھلائیں گے۔ کیونکہ وہی زیادہ مثمن کا منکر ہے۔ ہاں جو غلام ہلاک ہو چکا ہے بائع اس کے حصے کی قیمت کو چھوڑ دے اور ایسا محسوس ہو کہ جو زندہ غلام ہے وہی صرف مبیع ہے۔ ہلاک شدہ غلام گویا کہ مبیع نہیں ہے تب اس موجود غلام پر دونوں کو قسمیں کھلائیں گے۔ دونوں کو قسم کھلا کر بیع فسخ کرنا مقصود ہے اور بیع موجود غلام میں فسخ ہوگی ہلاک شدہ میں فسخ کریں تو اس کو واپس دینا ہوگا، ہلاک شدہ غلام کو واپس کیسے دیں؟ یہاں یہ صورت ہو سکتی ہے کہ ہلاک شدہ کو بائع بالکل بھول جائے اور صرف موجودہ غلام کو مبیع مانے تو اس صورت میں دونوں کو قسم کھلا کر بیع فسخ کریں اور موجود غلام کو بائع کی طرف واپس کریں [۲۷۹۲] (۶۳) امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ دونوں قسمیں کھائیں اور بیع فسخ ہوگی زندہ غلام میں اور ہلاک شدہ کی قیمت میں۔ امام ابو یوسف کا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جو زندہ ہے وہ بیع تو موجود ہے اس لئے اس میں بائع اور مشتری دونوں کو مدعی اور دونوں کو مدعی علیہ مان سکتے ہیں۔ اس میں دونوں کو قسمیں کھا کر بیع فسخ کریں گے۔ اور جو موجود غلام ہے اس کو واپس کرو اور جو ہلاک ہو چکا ہے اس کی قیمت واپس کرو۔ اصول امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک موجودہ غلام میں دونوں مدعی اور دونوں مدعی علیہ بن سکتے ہیں۔ اس لئے دونوں سے قسمیں بھی لی جاسکتی ہے اور اس کے تابع کر کے ہلاک شدہ کی قیمت میں بھی قسمیں لی جائے گی۔
[2791] (62) If one of the two slaves perishes, then they dispute about the price, they are not made to take mutual oaths according to Abu Hanifa, unless the seller agrees to forgo the share of the deceased. [2792] (63) And Abu Yusuf said: They take mutual oaths and the sale is rescinded regarding the living (slave) and the value of the deceased. The seller had sold two slaves, the buyer took possession, then one slave perished. ... The principle according to Abu Yusuf: in the living slave, both can be plaintiff and both defendant, so oaths can be taken from both, and as a subsidiary, also for the price of the deceased.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on a situation where two slaves are sold as a set for a single price, the buyer takes possession, and one slave perishes. Then a dispute arises about the total price. Abu Hanifa rules that no mutual oaths are taken initially because the buyer is the defendant regarding the price for the living slave. However, if the seller agrees to forgo the share of the deceased slave, the living slave alone becomes the subject, and then mutual oaths can be taken for that slave, leading to rescission. This is because the dead slave cannot be returned. Abu Yusuf, on the other hand, holds that mutual oaths are taken immediately regarding the living slave, and as a consequence, also for the value of the deceased. His principle is that since the living slave is present, both parties can be considered plaintiff and defendant for that item, and the value of the deceased is treated as part of the same dispute. The commentary explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa focuses on the impossibility of returning the dead slave, so he requires the seller to abandon that claim. Abu Yusuf sees the living slave as still capable of mutual claim.

Key Terms
حصہ (hissa): The share or portion of the price attributed to the deceased slave.
تابع (tābi‘): Subsidiary or dependent ruling – here, the ruling for the deceased slave follows the ruling for the living one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when can mutual oaths be taken if one of two slaves perishes?
A) Always
B) Only if the seller agrees to forgo the share of the deceased slave
C) Only if the buyer agrees to pay the market value
D) Never

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when part of a sold bundle perishes, the possibility of mutual oaths depends on whether the seller waives the claim on the lost item (Abu Hanifa) or treats the remaining item as the primary subject (Abu Yusuf).

Oath-Taking in Partial Destruction of Sold Item: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath requirement in partial destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on mutual oaths when one of two sold slaves dies, and apply the underlying principle of claimant/defendant to similar disputes.

Core Idea
When a sold item is partially destroyed, the ruling on oaths depends on whether the destroyed part is legally treated as still existing.

This passage exposes an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the requirement of mutual oaths (تحالف) when, after purchase, one of two slaves dies and the parties dispute the price. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that only the buyer takes an oath, while Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad require mutual oaths and consequent rescission. The dispute revolves around whether the dead slave's value is considered part of the sale subject matter. Understanding this nuance sharpens your ability to apply the general rule of claimant/defendant in sales disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Legal Question
Two slaves are sold; one dies after possession; then seller claims a higher price, buyer claims a lower one. The question: do they take mutual oaths or only the buyer?
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Principle
Abu Hanifa: no mutual oaths; only the buyer swears. Principle: after possession, seller is claimant (mudda'i), buyer is defendant (munkir). Oath is on defendant. The dead slave is no longer part of the sale.
If seller relinquishes the dead slave's share, then mutual oaths on the living slave.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Principle
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: mutual oaths taken; sale rescinded in living slave and value of dead. Principle: dead slave's value is treated as extant, so both parties are claimants and defendants regarding the total price.
4
Compare Practical Consequences (Thamarah al-Khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa: sale upheld at price buyer admits, unless seller relinquishes. Under Sahibayn: sale rescinded, buyer returns living slave and pays market value of dead one.
5
Recognize the Underlying Usul Difference
The difference stems from whether the destroyed item is considered legally existing (Sahibayn) or not (Abu Hanifa) for the purpose of the dispute. This reflects a broader principle about the status of destroyed property in legal disputes.

تعالی
The sale is on the value of the destroyed [slave]. If one of the two slaves died, then they disagreed on the price, they do not take oaths according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, unless the seller agrees to relinquish the share of the destroyed. (63) And Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, said: They take oaths and the sale is rescinded in the living [slave] and the value of the destroyed. [Urdu commentary: They give an oath to the denier. Therefore here also they will give an oath only to the buyer, and if he takes an oath, the decision will be according to his statement. The principle: This case is on the principle that after possession of the sold item, only the seller is the claimant and only the buyer is the defendant. Imam Muhammad says: They consider the value of the destroyed sold item as the original and the existing one as existing; in that case both can be claimants and both defendants. And since the dispute is in the amount of the price, therefore there is a dispute in the original contract. Therefore oaths will be taken from both and the sale will be rescinded, and the buyer will be told to return the market price of the sold item to the seller, as if the sale is annulled and the original sold item is returned. The principle of this school is that they consider the market price of the destroyed sold item as existing, and at the time of rescission, they return that price to the seller. [2791] (62) If one of the two slaves died, then they disputed the price, according to Imam Abu Hanifa they will not take oaths unless the seller agrees to relinquish the share of the destroyed. ... (then continuation) ... [2792] (63) Imam Abu Yusuf says: Both take oaths and the sale is rescinded in the living slave and in the value of the destroyed. His principle is that the living slave is present in the sale, therefore in it both the seller and buyer can be considered claimants and both defendants. In this, after both take oaths, the sale is rescinded. The living slave is returned and the value of the destroyed is returned. ... [2793] (64) And that is the saying of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths (تحالف) when one of two sold slaves dies before a price dispute arises. The core question is whether both parties take mutual oaths leading to rescission of the sale, or only the buyer takes an oath. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that after the buyer takes possession, the seller becomes the claimant (claiming the price) and the buyer becomes the defendant (denying the amount). Therefore, the burden of oath falls only on the defendant (buyer). However, if the seller relinquishes his claim on the dead slave's share, then the living slave alone is considered the subject matter, and both parties become claimants and defendants regarding it, so mutual oaths are taken. Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ. They treat the dead slave's value as still existing in the transaction. Since both parties dispute the total price, each has a claim: the seller claims a higher price, the buyer a lower one. Hence both are simultaneously claimants and defendants, requiring mutual oaths. After oaths, the sale is rescinded: the living slave is returned to the seller, and the buyer pays the market value of the dead slave. This difference stems from a fundamental principle: Abu Hanifa views the dead slave as no longer part of the sale, while the Sahibayn consider its value as a substitute. The text notes that Muhammad's view agrees with Abu Yusuf's (وهو قول محمد).

Key Terms
مدعی (mudda'ī): The claimant in a legal dispute; the party who asserts a right or obligation against another.
منکر (munkir): The defendant who denies the claim; the one who swears an oath if the claimant has no evidence.
یمین (yamīn): An oath sworn by a party in a dispute, usually by the defendant, to affirm his denial. In this context, mutual oaths (تحالف) refer to both parties swearing.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or rescission of a contract; here, the sale is voided due to the dispute resolved by mutual oaths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, in the case where one of two sold slaves dies before the price dispute, who takes the oath?
A) Both the seller and buyer take oaths.
B) Only the seller takes an oath.
C) Only the buyer takes an oath.
D) Neither takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the oath in price disputes after partial destruction hinges on whether the destroyed part is legally extant — Abu Hanifa treats it as out, the Sahibayn treat its value as a substitute.

The Ruling on Oaths When the Sold Item is Destroyed
States Imam Muhammad's ruling on mutual oaths for destroyed mabi‘.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's ruling on oaths when the sold item (mabi‘) is destroyed and apply it to scenarios of total and partial destruction.

Core Idea
When a sold item is destroyed, both parties must swear oaths – each is both claimant and defendant.

This passage from Al-Shareh al-Sameri presents a concise ruling from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani on the procedure when the sold commodity (mabi‘) perishes before delivery. The ruling establishes a mutual oath requirement, grounded in the Hanafi understanding of simultaneous claim and denial. By examining this ruling, we learn how the Hanafi school handles disputes over destroyed goods and how the principle extends by priority to partial destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine the legal context: the sold item (mabi‘) is destroyed before delivery, leading to a dispute between buyer and seller.
Destruction can be total or partial.
2
State the ruling
According to Imam Muhammad, both the buyer and the seller must take an oath (qasm). This is wajib (obligatory) to resolve the dispute.
The ruling is from the chapter of claims (al-qismah).
3
Understand the underlying principle
Both parties are simultaneously claimants and defendants. The buyer claims the seller is responsible, and the seller claims the buyer still owes the price. Each must swear to support their claim.
This principle distinguishes Hanafi law from some other schools.
4
Apply the a fortiori (qiyas al-awla)
If the rule applies when the entire item is destroyed, it applies with even greater reason when only half is destroyed. The commentary extends the ruling to partial destruction.
Also applies to destruction of part of the item.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The text only presents Imam Muhammad's view. Other Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf) may hold different opinions. This ikhtilaf is legitimate.
The text does not detail the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحى وقيمة الهالک (۲۷۹۳) (۶۴) وهو قول محمد رحمه الله تعالی
The living and the value of the destroyed (item). (2793) (64) This is the statement of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter of claims. It presents the view of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the case where the object of sale is destroyed. According to Imam Muhammad, when the entire sold item is destroyed, both the buyer and seller must take an oath. This ruling is based on the principle that both parties are simultaneously claimants and defendants. The commentary in Urdu explains that if half the item is destroyed (e.g., one of two slaves), then by priority (a fortiori) the same rule applies—both must take oaths.

Key Terms
هَالِك (halik): Destroyed (item) – the sold commodity that perishes before delivery.
قِسْمَة (qismah): Oath sworn by a litigant.
مَبِيع (mabi'): The sold item or commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, when the entire sold item (mabi‘) is destroyed before delivery, what is the ruling regarding oaths?
A) Only the buyer takes an oath.
B) Only the seller takes an oath.
C) Both parties take oaths.
D) No oath is required; the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when the mabi‘ is destroyed, Imam Muhammad requires mutual oaths because each party is both claimant and defendant.

Dividing Household Items Between Spouse and Heirs Upon Death
Rule for dividing household items between surviving spouse and deceased's heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for dividing household items when a spouse dies, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and apply the ruling to a given scenario.

Core Idea
Customary suitability and possession determine ownership; upon death, disputed joint-use items go to the survivor unless proof indicates otherwise.

When a spouse dies, household items often become a source of dispute between the surviving spouse and the deceased's heirs. Hanafi fiqh provides clear rules based on customary suitability and possession, with an important difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. This lesson will walk you through the general principle, the application upon death, and the practical consequences of the intra-madhhab dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for living spouses
Understand the default categories: items for men go to men, items for women go to women, items suitable for both go to the man.
2
Apply the rule to the death scenario
When one spouse dies, items suitable for both go to the survivor (the one still alive), unless there is a witness or qarina indicating otherwise.
The survivor is the spouse still living, not the deceased's heirs.
3
Analyze the proofs (athar) cited
Examine the reports from al-Hakam and Hammad that support the rule. Al-Hakam's report emphasises the exception for the wife proving ownership; Hammad's report emphasises possession.
These athar are from the Musannaf of Ibn Abi Shayba, used as evidence in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Compare Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions
Abu Hanifa: default rule applies, items suitable for both go to man (or his heirs) unless woman proves ownership. Abu Yusuf: items typically given as dowry (jihaz) go to the woman based on qarina; joint-use items without indication go to the man.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not between madhhabs.
5
Determine the practical outcome (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the wife has no proof, joint-use items go to the husband's heirs. Under Abu Yusuf, dowry items go to the wife regardless, and only truly ambiguous items go to the husband's heirs.
The mufta bihi is not specified in this text; both views have weight.

Household items: what is suitable for men goes to men, what is suitable for women goes to women, and what is suitable for both goes to the man. (73) If one of them dies and his heirs dispute with the other (survivor), then what is suitable for both men and women goes to the survivor. (74) Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It will be given (to the wife). This is when there is no indication (qarina) and no witness that it belongs to the woman. (3) There is proof of this in the narration from al-Hakam who said: When a man dies and leaves household items, what is for men is not for women, what is for women is not for men; it is for the woman; and what is for men and women is for the man unless the woman brings proof that it is hers. (a) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 226, on a man who divorces or dies and there are items in his house, vol. 4 p. 188 no. 19134) In this athar: what is suitable for men is for men, what is suitable for women is for women, and what is suitable for both is for the man. (2802) (73) So if one of them dies and his heirs dispute with the other, then what is suitable for men and women is for the survivor. For example, the man dies and the wife is alive. Now the man's heirs dispute with the wife about the household items. Then what is suitable for men will go to the man's heirs, what is suitable for women will go to the woman, and what is suitable for both when there is no witness or clear indication, then it will go to the woman. He who died has lost possession, and there is no one opposing his wife. Those things come into the wife's possession, so they will go to the woman. (2) There is an athar from Hammad: He was asked about household items and said: The woman's clothes are for the woman, the man's clothes are for the man, and what they dispute about, being neither for this nor that, is for the one in whose hand it is. (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba, 326, on a man who divorces or dies and there are items in his house, vol. 4 p. 188 no. 19131) In this athar: what is in whose possession is for him. And after the husband dies, those things are in the wife's possession, so they are for the wife. 2803 (74) Imam Abu Yusuf said: The wife will be given those things that are given as dowry, and the rest will be for the husband. He says: The clear indication is that dowry items are brought from her parental home and are the woman's, so all things that are given as dowry will be given to the woman, also clothes suitable for women. And items for which there is no witness or indication will be for the husband even if he has died. Footnote: (a) Hakam said: If a man dies and leaves household items, then what is for men is not for women, what is for women is not for men, it is for the woman; and what is for both is for the man unless the woman affirms that it is specifically hers. (b) Hammad was asked about household items. He said: The woman's clothes are for the woman, the man's clothes are for the man, and what they dispute about, neither for this nor that, is for the one in whose hand it is.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on dividing household items between spouses or their heirs upon death. The general principle (from the matn) is that items are assigned based on customary suitability: men's items go to men, women's items to women, and items suitable for both go to the man. The commentator then expands on the case of death: if one spouse dies and the surviving spouse disputes with the deceased's heirs, items suitable for both go to the survivor. The Urdu commentary explains that this applies when there is no qarina (indication) or witness. It then cites athar from al-Hakam and Hammad supporting these rules. A significant intra-Hanafi dispute is then introduced: Abu Yusuf holds that items typically given as dowry (jihaz) go to the wife, and the rest to the husband (or his heirs), based on the indication that such items come from the wife's family. This contrasts with the default rule that items suitable for both go to the man. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but the text gives weight to both views.

Key Terms
متاع البيت (matāʿ al-bayt): Household items and furnishings collectively.
قرینہ (qarīnah): An external indication or circumstance that helps determine ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband dies and his heirs dispute with the wife over a carpet suitable for both men and women, and there is no witness or indication, who gets the carpet?
A) The wife
B) The husband's heirs
C) It is divided equally
D) It goes to the state

Key Takeaway
Upon a spouse's death, items suitable for both genders go to the surviving spouse unless proof of ownership indicates otherwise; Abu Yusuf adds that dowry items go to the wife regardless.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Claim of Child from Sold Slave-Girl Within Six Months
Ruling on paternity claim by seller when slave-girl gives birth soon after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a seller claims a child born within six months of selling a slave-girl, and explain the Quranic derivation of the six-month minimum pregnancy.

Core Idea
Six months from birth to sale proves paternity—minimum pregnancy deduced from Quranic arithmetic.

This lesson examines a specific case in Hanafi sales law: a seller claims paternity when a sold slave-girl gives birth within six months. The ruling protects the seller's lineage and establishes the slave-girl as umm walad, invalidating the sale. The proof is a beautiful example of Quranic deduction combined with a Companion precedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
First, understand the situation: a man sells a slave-girl, then she gives birth. The seller claims the child. The text states that if the birth occurs within six months of the sale, the child belongs to the seller, the slave-girl becomes umm walad, and the sale is rescinded.
Note: the six months are calculated from the day of sale, not from conception.
2
Understand the legal consequence
The child is attributed to the seller, making the slave-girl his umm walad. Since an umm walad cannot be sold, the sale becomes invalid and the price must be returned. This shows how ownership and paternity intersect.
3
Examine the proof for minimum pregnancy
The text uses two Quranic verses: Surah al-Baqarah 2:233 (suckling for two full years) and Surah al-Ahqaf 46:15 (total pregnancy and weaning is thirty months). Combining them: 30 months total - 24 months suckling = 6 months minimum pregnancy.
This deduction is unique to Hanafi usul (combining verses to derive a ruling).
4
Analyze the Companion precedent
The story of Umar and Ali: Umar wanted to stone a woman who gave birth at six months (implying zina), but Ali explained using the verses that six months is the minimum, so the child could be legitimate. This confirms the six-month rule as a basis for legal decisions.
The athar is cited as evidence (dalil) in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the rule to the given case
Since the birth occurred within six months of the sale, the seller's claim is valid. The child is his, the mother becomes umm walad, and the sale is rescinded. The seller returns the price.
If the seller does not claim, the child belongs to the buyer (default rule).

And if a man sells a slave-girl, and she comes with a child, and the seller claims it, if she gives birth to it in less than six months from the day he sold her, then it is the son of the seller, and its mother becomes umm walad for the seller, and the sale is rescinded, and the price is returned. The passage also repeats the earlier household items ruling in Urdu and then continues: The minimum period of pregnancy is six months, and the evidence for this is the athar: Umar was brought a woman who had given birth at six months and he intended to stone her. Then Ali was informed, and he said there is no stoning on her. Umar heard and sent for him and asked. Ali said: 'And mothers shall suckle their children for two whole years, for those who desire to complete the suckling' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:233) 'and his weaning is in thirty months' (Surah al-Ahqaf 46:15). So six months is the period of pregnancy, and two years completes the suckling. So there is no stoning on her. He said: Then he released her. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on the minimum period of pregnancy, vol. 7 p. 727 no. 15548) This athar is the proof.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding a sold slave-girl who gives birth within six months of the sale. If the seller claims the child as his own, the child is attributed to him, and the slave-girl becomes his umm walad (mother of his child). Consequently, the sale is invalidated because a man cannot sell his umm walad, and the seller must return the purchase price to the buyer. The reasoning is based on the minimum duration of pregnancy being six months, as derived from the Quranic verses combined: full suckling is two years (24 months), and total pregnancy and weaning is 30 months, leaving 6 months as the minimum pregnancy. The text cites the famous incident of Umar and Ali to confirm this principle: a woman who gave birth at six months after marriage was not subject to the hadd penalty because the child could be legitimate. The ruling demonstrates the Hanafi method of combining Quranic verses to derive legal durations. The rule applies only when the seller claims the child; if he does not claim, the child belongs to the buyer, as the default is that the child follows the mother's owner at time of birth.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne a child to her master, thereby gaining freedom upon his death and becoming unsellable.
أقل مدة الحمل (aqall muddat al-haml): The minimum duration of pregnancy, established as six months by combining Quranic verses.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
