Partition Rescission for Defective Shares: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
States the intra-Hanafi dispute on partition rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on partition rescission when a share is defective, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A defective share does not automatically void partition; Imam Abu Hanifa requires compensation, while Imam Abu Yusuf rescinds the partition.

When partners divide property and later discover that part of one partner's share actually belongs to a third party, a dispute arises: should the partition stand (with compensation) or be voided? This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, focusing on the legal reasoning behind each position and the practical consequences for the partners.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa (partition stands, partner recovers from partner's share) and Abu Yusuf (partition is rescinded).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both imams are within the school.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's reasoning
The text explains that the partner (Zayd) consented based on full ownership of his assigned share. When part is taken by a third party, the share is smaller and the replacement comes from another location, potentially causing loss or dissatisfaction. Hence, lack of true consent voids the partition.
Consent (rida) is a key element in contracts of exchange.
3
Consider the missing Hanafi response
The text does not provide Abu Hanifa's full dalil, but we can infer that he treats the partition as a final exchange; defects are compensated monetarily rather than voiding the contract.
This reflects the principle that a contract is not voided by a remediable defect.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the original partition remains valid and the affected partner gets compensation from the other partner's share. Under Abu Yusuf, the partition is rescinded and a new division must be made.
The practical outcome differs: stability vs. full consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجع بحصة ذلك من نصيب شريكه وقال ابو يوسف تفسخ القسمة.
ان امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک تقسیم ٹوٹ جائے گی اب دوبارہ تقسیم کرائے۔
زید نے تقسیم کے وقت سمجھا تھا کہ یہ پورا حصہ میرا ہے۔ اب عمر کے لینے کی وجہ سے وہ حصہ چھوٹا ہو گیا جس کی وجہ سے ہو سکتا ہے کہ اس کو نقصان ہو۔ اور خالد سے جو حصہ ملے گا وہ دوسری جگہ سے ملیگا جس پر ممکن ہے کہ گھانا ہو اس لئے اس عدم رضامندی کی وجہ سے تقسیم ٹوٹ جائے گی۔
He returns with the share of that from the portion of his partner, and Abu Yusuf said: The partition is rescinded. Indeed, according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the partition will break; now a new partition will be arranged. Zayd thought at the time of partition that this entire share was his. Now due to 'Umar's taking, that share became smaller, which may cause him loss. And the share he gets from Khalid will come from another place, which may result in loss. Therefore, due to this lack of consent, the partition will be rescinded.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding partition (qismah). The text presents two positions: the first, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that if after partition it is discovered that a portion of one partner's share actually belongs to a third party (e.g., a creditor), that partner does not get the partition voided but instead takes compensation from the other partner's share. The second position is of Imam Abu Yusuf: the entire partition is rescinded because the partner was under a misapprehension about full ownership; now that a part is taken, he may suffer loss or be dissatisfied with the replacement share from a different location. Therefore, due to lack of consent, the partition is voided and a new one is required. The text does not mention Imam Muhammad's position nor specify which is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قسمة (qismah): Partition or division of jointly owned property among partners.
تفسخ (tunqad): Is rescinded or voided.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, what happens to a partition when one partner's share is later found to belong to a third party?
A) The partition remains valid.
B) The partition is rescinded.
C) Compensation is paid.
D) The third party is given a share.

Key Takeaway
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, the partition remains with compensation; under Abu Yusuf, it is rescinded requiring a new division.

Coercion in Contracts: The Hanafi Rule of Option
Ruling on contracts made under coercion: the coerced party has an option to annul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contracts made under coercion and distinguish between contracts that are binding under coercion and those that give an option to annul.

Core Idea
Coercion does not void a contract; it gives the coerced party an option to rescind, except for marriage, divorce, and emancipation.

This passage from the Hanafi text addresses a common yet legally nuanced scenario: contracts entered under duress. While coercion undermines free consent, Hanafi law does not automatically void such contracts. Instead, it categorizes legal acts into two types: those that are effective regardless of consent (like divorce) and those that require consent for finality (like sale). For the latter, the coerced party has a choice to uphold or annul the contract. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of ikrah in muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the coerced acts mentioned
List the acts: sale, purchase, acknowledgment (iqrar), and hire (ijarah). Note the types of coercion: threat of death, severe beating, imprisonment.
These are 'uqud al-mu'awada (exchange contracts).
2
State the ruling for these acts
The coerced party has an option (ikhtiyar) to either affirm the contract or rescind it. The contract is valid but not final until the option is exercised.
The option is not indefinite; it should be exercised promptly to avoid indication of consent.
3
Distinguish acts that are binding under coercion
Marriage (nikah), divorce (talaq), revocation (raj'ah), and emancipation (itq) are effective immediately upon utterance, even under coercion. The passage provides this exception.
These acts become effective by mere utterance, so coercion does not grant an option.
4
Understand the underlying principle
Contracts that take effect by mere utterance (like marriage) are binding under coercion; contracts that require consent (like sale) give an option. This is based on the nature of the act.
The principle is rooted in the idea that for some legal acts, the utterance itself produces the effect (inqi'ād), while for others, true consent is a condition for finality (luzūm).
5
Apply the rule to practical examples
Confirm that the four mentioned acts (sale, purchase, iqrar, ijarah) fall into the second category. For instance, a coerced sale: the seller can later rescind by returning the price.
If the coerced party affirms, the contract becomes fully binding from the start.

Source Text

برقرار رکھے چاہے بیچ توڑ کر مبیع واپس کرے۔ مجبور کر کے کوئی سامان خریدواد یا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہوگا چاہے بیچ برقرار رکھے اور چاہے تو ضح کر دے اور اپنا شمن واپس لے لے۔ مجبور کر کے اقرار کر وایا تو اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے اقرار پر برقرار ر ہے چاہے انکار کر دے۔ مجبور کر کے گھر کو اجرت پر دلوایا تو اس کو اختیار ہے کہ
If a man is coerced to sell his property, or to buy a commodity, or to acknowledge a debt of one thousand dirhams, or to rent his house, and he is coerced by threat of killing, severe beating, or imprisonment, and he sells or buys, then he has the choice: if he wishes he may affirm the contract, or if he wishes he may rescind it.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of coercion on contracts that involve exchange of property or services (uqud al-mu'awada). The author states that if a person is coerced into selling, buying, making an acknowledgment, or renting, and the coercion involves a credible threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment, then the coerced party has an option: he may either uphold the contract or annul it. The key distinction made here is between two types of contracts: those that become effective immediately upon utterance regardless of consent (such as marriage, divorce, revocation ('iddah), and emancipation) and those that require mutual consent and can be annulled (such as sale, purchase, acknowledgment, and hire). For the first type, coercion does not prevent the contract from being binding; they are effective even if uttered in jest, so coercion also makes them binding. For the second type, coercion renders the contract effective but gives the coerced party a choice to rescind. The passage explains the underlying principle: contracts that are effective by mere utterance are binding under coercion, while contracts that require consent give an option when done under coercion. The examples clarify that sale, purchase, acknowledgment, and hire fall into the second category. This is a fundamental distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding the impact of ikrah on different types of legal acts.

Key Terms
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): The right of the coerced party to either confirm or rescind a contract made under coercion.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment of the contract, returning both parties to their pre-contract state.
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrāh al-mulji'): Coercion that overcomes resistance, such as threats of death, severe beating, or imprisonment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Selling a house.
B) Purchasing a commodity.
C) Divorcing one's wife.
D) Renting a house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, coerced contracts of exchange are valid but rescindable, while acts like marriage and divorce are binding even under coercion.

The Hanafi Ruling on Coerced Contracts: Valid but Non-Binding
Ruling with proof from Quran and athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced commercial contracts, identify qualifying duress, and apply the rule that subsequent voluntary acceptance makes the contract binding.

Core Idea
A coerced contract is valid but not binding – the victim has the option to cancel or confirm.

In Hanafi fiqh, a contract made under genuine duress is not automatically void. Instead, the school distinguishes between the act of the contract (offer and acceptance) and the consent behind it. This lesson unpacks that distinction, shows what counts as duress, and explains when the coerced party can walk away or uphold the deal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage to determine what act is being ruled upon (selling, buying, leasing, acknowledging under duress) and the hukm: the contract is sahih (valid) but not lazim (binding). The coerced party has a khiyar (option) to uphold or rescind.
This is the foundational ruling of the passage.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proofs cited: Quran 4:29 (trade by mutual consent) and the athar of Shuraih (imprisonment, beating, shackling, threat are duress). Understand that these establish the requirement of consent for bindingness.
The Quran provides the general principle; the athar defines ikrah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the proofs yield the Hanafi ruling. The phrase 'عن تراض' (by mutual consent) implies that without consent, the contract is not invalid but consumption of wealth is prohibited; thus the contract stands but is defeasible. The athar scales the threat level needed to disable consent.
The Hanafi position hinges on the difference between validity (siḥḥah) and bindingness (luzūm).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Compare with the Shafi'i school, which holds coerced contracts void (bāṭil) because consent is a condition of validity. The Hanafis respond that a sane adult's utterance alone creates a valid contract; consent only affects bindingness.
This ikhtilaf is a classic example of differing usul principles.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is the relied-upon (muftā bihi) view in the school. The passage also clarifies that if the coerced party later voluntarily accepts the price or benefit, that constitutes ratification and makes the contract binding.
Subsequent voluntary action removes the defect of duress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الاكراه
او لصا [۲۹۶۶](۲) واذا أكره الرجل على بيع ماله او على شراء سلعة او على ان يقر لرجل بالف درهم او يـواجـر داره وأكره على ذلك بالقتل او بالضرب الشديد او بالحبس فباع او اشترى فهو بالخيار ان شاء امضى البيع وان شاء فسخه ورجع بالمبيع [۲۹](۳) فان كان قبض الثمن طوعا فقد اجاز البيع وان كان قبضه مكرها فليس باجازة وعليه ردّه ان كان قائما في يده.
ثم شرح: اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ صرف بادشاہ ہی کی جانب سے اکراہ ہوسکتا ہے۔
نت توعد : وعد سے مشتق ہے، دھمکی دے، لص : چور۔
۲۹۶۶) (۲) اگر کسی آدمی کو مجبور کیا گیا اپنے مال کے بیچنے پر یا سامان خرید نے پر یا کسی آدمی کے لئے ہزار درہم کے اقرار کرنے پر یا اپنے گھر کو اجرت پر رکھنے پر یا مجبور کیا اس کو اس پر قتل کی دھمکی دے کر یا سخت مار کی یا قید کرنے کی ، پس بیچ دیا یا خریدا تو اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے بیع باقی رکھے اور چاہے اس کو فسخ کر دے اور بیع واپس لے لے۔
کسی آدمی کو مجبور کیا کہ وہ اپنا مال بیچ دے۔ یا کوئی سامان خریدے۔ یا کسی آدمی کے لئے ہزار درہم کا اقرار کرے۔ یا اپنے گھر کو اجرت پر رکھے۔ اور مجبور بھی کیا قتل کرنے کی دھمکی دے کر یا سخت مار کی دھمکی دے کر یا قید کرنے کی دھمکی دے کر ۔ اس نے ان مجبوریوں کی وجہ سے سامان بیچ دیا یا خرید لیا تو یہ خریدنا پکا نہیں ہے۔ بلکہ اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے تو بیع اور شراء اور اجرت برقرار رکھے یا چاہے تو ڑ دے اور مبیع واپس لے لے اور اجرت کی چیز واپس لے لے۔
ان مسائل کا قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ جو عقد ایسا ہو کہ زبان سے نکلتے ہی جاری ہو جاتا ہو چاہے خوشی سے زبان سے نکالے یا مذاق سے نکالے یا کسی کے مجبور کرنے سے نکالے۔ ایسے عقود مجبور کرنے سے بھی کرے تو واقع ہو جائیں گے اور دوبارہ تو ڑ بھی نہیں سکتا۔ جیسے نکاح ، طلاق، رجعت ، آزاد کرنا۔ یہ سب کام کسی کے مجبور کرنے سے کیا تب بھی واقع ہو جائیں گے۔ مثلا کسی کے مجبور کرنے سے طلاق دیا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی۔ یا کسی کے مجبور کرنے سے نکاح کیا تو نکاح ہو جائے گا۔ یا کسی کے مجبور کرنے سے رجعت کی تو رجعت ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ یہ مذاق سے بھی بولے تو رجعت ہو جاتی ہے، طلاق پڑ جاتی ہے اور نکاح ہو جاتا ہے اور آزادگی بھی واقع ہو جاتی ہے۔
پہلے کتاب الطلاق میں دلائل گزر چکے ہیں۔ اور ایسے عقد جو زبان سے نکلتے ہی واقع نہیں ہوتا بلکہ راضی خوشی سے عقد کرے تب عقد ہوتا ہے اور بعد میں فسخ کرے تو ضیح بھی ہو جاتا ہے۔ ایسا عقد مجبور اور اکراہ کر کے کرائے تو عقد تو ہو جائے گا لیکن عقد کرنے والے کو اختیار ہوگا کہ چاہے تو اس کو برقرار رکھے اور چاہے تو اس کو توڑ دے۔ متن کے چاروں عقد ایسے ہی ہیں۔ مثلا مجبور کر کے بیع کروایا تو بائع کو اختیار ہوگا چاہے بیچ برقرار رکھے چاہے بیچ توڑ کر مبیع واپس کرے۔ مجبور کر کے کوئی سامان خرید واد یا تو مشتری کو اختیار ہوگا چاہے بیچ برقرار رکھے اور چاہے تو ضح کر دے اور اپنا شمن واپس لے لے۔ مجبور کر کے اقرار کر وایا تو اس کو اختیار ہے چاہے اقرار پر برقرار ر ہے چاہے انکار کر دے۔ مجبور کر کے گھر کو اجرت پر دلوایا تو اس کو اختیار ہے کہ اجرت برقرار رکھے اور چاہے تو اجرت تو ڑ دے۔ کیونکہ یہ عقد رضامندی کے بغیر نہیں ہوئے (۲) آیت میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ یا ایھا الذین آمنوا لا تأكلوا اموالكم بينكم بالباطل الا ان تكون تجارة عن تراض منكم (الف) (آیت ۲۹، سورۃ النساء ۴ ) اس آیت میں ہے کہ تجارت رضامندی سے ہو تو واقع ہوگی اور رضامندی سے نہ ہو تو وہ مال باطل کے درجے میں ہے اس کا کھانا حرام ہے۔ اور زبردستی کر کے بیع و شراء کروایا تو رضا مندی نہیں ہوئی اس لئے عقد تو ہو جائے گا لیکن اس کو برقرار رکھنا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جو عقد رضامندی سے کرنے کا ہے اس میں زبردستی کرے تو عقد ہو جائے گا لیکن لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ عقد ہواس لئے جائے گا کہ عاقل بالغ آدمی نے ایجاب اور قبول کیا ہے۔ ایسے مواقع کے لئے تھوڑی بہت دھمکی سے اکراہ نہیں سمجھیں گے۔ ہاں ! نہ کرنے پرقتل کی دھمکی ہو، بے تحاشہ مار کی دھمکی ہو یا بہت دنوں تک قید کی دھمکی ہو تو اس کو اکراہ سمجھیں گے۔
اس کے لئے اثر گزر چکا ہے۔ عن شريح قال الحبس كره، والضرب كره والقيد كره والوعيد كره (ب) (سنن بیہقی،
باب ما یکون اکراها، ج سابع جس ۵۸۸ نمبر ۱۵۱۰۸ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب طلاق الکرہ، ج سادس بص ۴۱۱ نمبر ۱۱۴۲۳) سلحة : سامان، الحبس : قید، یواجر : اجرت پر دینا۔ [۲۹۷۷] (۳) پس اگر بائع نے شمن پر بخوشی قبضہ کیا تو گویا کہ بیع کو جائز قرار دے دیا، اور اگر زبردستی میں قبضہ کیا تو یہ اجازت نہیں ہوگی۔ اور اس پر اس کا لوٹانا ہوگا اگر اس کے پاس موجود ہو۔
بائع نے بیع مجبور کرنے کی وجہ سے کی۔ اب شمن پر قبضہ کرنے کا مرحلہ آیا تو دیکھا جائے گا کہ خوشی سے ثمن پر قبضہ کرتا ہے یا مجبوری کے درجے میں، اگر مجبور کرنے کی وجہ سے کی تب تو بیچ ٹوٹ جائے گی۔ کیونکہ بیع بھی اکراہ سے کیا اور ثمن پر قبضہ بھی اکراہ سے کیا۔ اس صورت میں اگر شمن اس کے پاس موجود ہو تو واپس کر دے۔ اور اگر شمن پر قبضہ خوشی سے کیا تو یہ اجازت کبھی جائے گی اور بیع کرتے وقت اگر چہ مجبوری تھی
لیکن ابھی اجازت ہوگئی اس لئے اب بیچ درست ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ اب رضامندی ہوگئی۔
الوں یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ بعد میں بھی رضا مندی ہو جائے تو کافی ہے۔ طوعا : خوشی سے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) اے ایمان والو! تم تمہارا مال آپس میں باطل طریقے پر مت کھاؤ۔ ہاں ! مگر یہ کہ تمہاری رضا مندی سے باہم تجارت ہو ( ب ) حضرت شریح فرماتے ہیں کہ جیل کرنا اکراہ ہے۔ اور مارنا اکراہ ہے اور بیڑی لگانا اکراہ ہے اور دھمکی دینا اکراہ ہے۔
The Book of Coercion: First, when a man is coerced into selling his property, or purchasing goods, or making an acknowledgment of a thousand dirhams to a man, or leasing his house, and he is coerced into that by threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment, and he sells or purchases, then he has the choice: if he wishes, he may uphold the sale, and if he wishes, he may rescind it and take back the sold item. If he accepted the price voluntarily, then he has validated the sale; but if he accepted it under duress, then it is not a validation, and he must return it if it remains in his hand. Then the explanation: From this report, it is understood that coercion can only come from a ruler. 'توعّد' is derived from 'وعد' (promise), meaning threat; 'لص' means thief. (2) If a person is coerced to sell his property, or to purchase goods, or to acknowledge a thousand dirhams for a person, or to lease his house, and he is coerced by threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment, and he sells or purchases, then he has the option: if he wishes, he may keep the sale, and if he wishes, he may cancel it and take back the sale item. If a person is coerced to sell his property... So he sold or purchased due to these coercions, then this purchase is not firm; rather, he has the option: if he wishes, he may keep the sale, purchase, and lease, or if he wishes, he may cancel and take back the sold item and the leased item. The rule for these issues is that a contract which becomes effective immediately upon utterance, whether uttered willingly, jokingly, or under coercion – such contracts even if made under coercion will be effective and cannot be undone later, like marriage, divorce, return, and emancipation. All these acts, if done under coercion, will still take effect. For example, if a man gives a divorce under coercion, the divorce occurs; if he marries under coercion, the marriage takes place; if he returns (his wife) under coercion, the return takes place. Because even if said jokingly, the return occurs, divorce falls, marriage happens, and emancipation occurs. The proofs have already passed in the Book of Divorce. As for contracts that do not become effective immediately upon utterance but only when the contract is made willingly – then if it is rescinded later, it can be canceled. Such a contract, if made under coercion and duress, will occur, but the one who made it has the option to keep it or break it. The four contracts in the text are like this. For example, if a sale is made under coercion, the seller has the option to keep the sale or break it and take back the sold item. If goods are purchased under coercion, the buyer has the option to keep the purchase or cancel and take back his money. If an acknowledgment is made under coercion, he has the option to uphold the acknowledgment or deny it. If a house is leased under coercion, he has the option to keep the lease or cancel it. Because these contracts are not made without consent. (2) Proof of this is in the verse: 'O you who believe! Do not consume your property among yourselves wrongfully, unless it be a trade done by mutual consent.' (Surah an-Nisa 4:29). This verse states that trade becomes actual when based on consent; if not based on consent, the property is deemed false and consuming it is prohibited. When sale and purchase are made by force, there is no consent, so the contract will occur but it is not necessary to keep it. The principle of this issue is that for a contract that requires consent, if force is used, the contract will occur but will not be binding. The contract occurs because a sane adult made the offer and acceptance. For these situations, a slight threat is not considered coercion; yes, if there is a threat of death, excessive beating, or long imprisonment, it is considered coercion. There is a report for this: 'Shuraih said: Imprisonment is duress, beating is duress, shackling is duress, and threat is duress.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab ma yakunu ikrahan). 'Sul'a' means goods, 'al-habs' means imprisonment, 'yu'ajir' means to give on rent. (3) Then if the seller accepted the price voluntarily, he has validated the sale; and if he accepted it under duress, it is not a validation, and he must return it if it remains in his hand. The seller made the sale due to coercion. Now when the stage of accepting the price comes, it will be seen whether he accepts the price willingly or under duress. If he accepted it due to duress, then the sale will break. Because the sale was made under duress and the price was also accepted under duress. In that case, if the price is still with him, he should return it. And if he accepted the price willingly, then this constitutes validation, and even if at the time of sale there was duress, now validation has occurred, so the sale becomes correct. Because now consent has occurred. This issue is based on the rule that later consent is sufficient. 'Taw'an' means willingly. Footnote: (a) O you who believe! Do not consume your property among yourselves wrongfully, unless it be a trade done by mutual consent. (b) Shuraih said: Imprisonment is duress, beating is duress, shackling is duress, and threat is duress.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is from the section on coercion (ikrah) and explains the rulings on contracts made under duress. The text first lists the contracts: sale, purchase, acknowledgment of debt, and lease. It then specifies that qualifying duress is threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment. If a person is coerced into any of these contracts, the contract is valid (sahih) but the coerced party has the option (khiyar) to either uphold or cancel it. This is because the act (offer and acceptance) was performed by a sane adult, so the contract comes into existence, but since consent (rida) is lacking, it is not binding (lazim). The text then distinguishes contracts that are binding immediately upon utterance, even if made jokingly or under duress, such as marriage, divorce, return, and emancipation. For these, coercion does not nullify them because they are effective on utterance alone. The proof for the non-binding nature of coerced commercial contracts is Quran 4:29, which requires mutual consent. Additionally, the report of Shuraih defines what constitutes coercion. The final part discusses the effect of subsequently accepting the price voluntarily: if the coerced seller later accepts the price willingly, that constitutes ratification (ijazah) of the sale, and the contract becomes binding. If he accepts it under duress again, it is not ratification, and he must return the price if it still exists.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against their will, specifically threats of death, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment that overpower the will.
اختيار (khiyar): The option/rescission right given to a coerced party to either uphold or cancel the contract.
رضا (rida): Consent; inner contentment without which a contract is not binding, though the contract is still valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following contracts remains binding even if made under duress?
A) A sale of property
B) A marriage contract
C) A lease of a house
D) A purchase of goods

Key Takeaway
Under Hanafi law, a coerced commercial contract is valid but not binding; the coerced party has the option to cancel or confirm, and subsequent voluntary consent makes it binding.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Coercion in Sale by Imprisonment
Ruling on sale under coercion and its effect on validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale made under coercion, identify the effect of voluntary acceptance of the price, and explain the proof from Quran 4:29.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is valid but non-binding; voluntary acceptance of the price ratifies it.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from the Book of Coercion: what happens when someone is forced by imprisonment to sell or buy? The Hanafi school distinguishes between the contract's validity and its binding nature. Even though offer and acceptance are present, the lack of genuine consent means the contract is not immediately binding. We will analyze the proof from the Quran and explore how later voluntary action can ratify the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale or purchase made by a person coerced by imprisonment. The ruling: the coerced party has the option (khiyar) to either uphold the sale or annul it.
The contract is technically valid because offer and acceptance occur, but it is non-binding (ghayr lazim).
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 4:29: 'O you who believe, do not consume your wealth among yourselves in falsehood, except that it be a trade by mutual consent.'
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah regarding the ruling on coercion.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'by mutual consent' (an taradin) indicates that consent is a necessary condition for a binding trade. A coerced transaction lacks genuine consent, so it is not binding initially, but the contract is not false (batil) because offer and acceptance exist.
The Hanafi interpretation: the verse prohibits consuming wealth without consent, but the contract itself is not absolutely false.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that a coerced sale is invalid (batil) because consent is a condition for validity, not just bindingness.
The Hanafi school distinguishes between validity (sihha) and bindingness (luzum).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is as stated in the matn: the coerced party has the option, and voluntary acceptance of the price after coercion ends ratifies the contract.
This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع

كتاب الاكراه
بالحبس فباع او اشترى فهو بالخيار ان شاء امضى البيع وان شاء فسخه ورجع بالمبيع [۲۹](۳) فان كان قبض الثمن طوعا فقد اجاز البيع وان كان قبضه مكرها فليس باجازة وعليه ردّه ان كان قائما في يده.
اجرت برقرار رکھے اور چاہے تو اجرت تو ڑ دے۔ کیونکہ یہ عقد رضامندی کے بغیر نہیں ہوئے (۲) آیت میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ یا ایھا الذین آمنوا لا تأكلوا اموالكم بينكم بالباطل الا ان تكون تجارة عن تراض منكم (الف) (آیت ۲۹، سورۃ النساء ۴ ) اس آیت میں ہے کہ تجارت رضامندی سے ہو تو واقع ہوگی اور رضامندی سے نہ ہو تو وہ مال باطل کے درجے میں ہے اس کا کھانا حرام ہے۔ اور زبردستی کر کے بیع و شراء کروایا تو رضا مندی نہیں ہوئی اس لئے عقد تو ہو جائے گا لیکن اس کو برقرار رکھنا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ اصول یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ جو عقد رضامندی سے کرنے کا ہے اس میں زبردستی کرے تو عقد ہو جائے گا لیکن لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ عقد ہواس لئے جائے گا کہ عاقل بالغ آدمی نے ایجاب اور قبول کیا ہے۔ ایسے مواقع کے لئے تھوڑی بہت دھمکی سے اکراہ نہیں سمجھیں گے۔ ہاں ! نہ کرنے پرقتل کی دھمکی ہو، بے تحاشہ مار کی دھمکی ہو یا بہت دنوں تک قید کی دھمکی ہو تو اس کو اکراہ سمجھیں گے۔
اس کے لئے اثر گزر چکا ہے۔ عن شريح قال الحبس كره، والضرب كره والقيد كره والوعيد كره (ب) (سنن بیہقی،
باب ما یکون اکراها، ج سابع جس ۵۸۸ نمبر ۱۵۱۰۸ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب طلاق الکرہ، ج سادس بص ۴۱۱ نمبر ۱۱۴۲۳) سلحة : سامان، الحبس : قید، یواجر : اجرت پر دینا۔ [۲۹۷۷] (۳) پس اگر بائع نے شمن پر بخوشی قبضہ کیا تو گویا کہ بیع کو جائز قرار دے دیا، اور اگر زبردستی میں قبضہ کیا تو یہ اجازت نہیں ہوگی۔ اور اس پر اس کا لوٹانا ہوگا اگر اس کے پاس موجود ہو۔
بائع نے بیع مجبور کرنے کی وجہ سے کی۔ اب شمن پر قبضہ کرنے کا مرحلہ آیا تو دیکھا جائے گا کہ خوشی سے ثمن پر قبضہ کرتا ہے یا مجبوری کے درجے میں، اگر مجبور کرنے کی وجہ سے کی تب تو بیچ ٹوٹ جائے گی۔ کیونکہ بیع بھی اکراہ سے کیا اور ثمن پر قبضہ بھی اکراہ سے کیا۔ اس صورت میں اگر شمن اس کے پاس موجود ہو تو واپس کر دے۔ اور اگر شمن پر قبضہ خوشی سے کیا تو یہ اجازت کبھی جائے گی اور بیع کرتے وقت اگر چہ مجبوری تھی
لیکن ابھی اجازت ہوگئی اس لئے اب بیچ درست ہو جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ اب رضامندی ہوگئی۔
الوں یہ مسئلہ اس اصول پر ہے کہ بعد میں بھی رضا مندی ہو جائے تو کافی ہے۔ طوعا : خوشی سے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) اے ایمان والو! تم تمہارا مال آپس میں باطل طریقے پر مت کھاؤ۔ ہاں ! مگر یہ کہ تمہاری رضا مندی سے باہم تجارت ہو ( ب ) حضرت شریح فرماتے ہیں کہ جیل کرنا اکراہ ہے۔ اور مارنا اکراہ ہے اور بیڑی لگانا اکراہ ہے اور دھمکی دینا اکراہ ہے۔
Al-Sharh al-Thamiri, Part Four, page 224. Book of Coercion. If one is imprisoned and sells or buys, he has the option: if he wishes, he confirms the sale; if he wishes, he annuls it and takes back the sold item. If he voluntarily took the price, he has validated the sale; if he took it under coercion, it is not validation, and he must return it if it remains in his hand. ... (explanation with Quran 4:29 and hadith from Shurayh: 'Imprisonment is coercion, beating is coercion, shackling is coercion, and threatening is coercion.') (3) If the seller voluntarily took the price, it is as if he ratified the sale; if he took it under coercion, it is not permission, and he must return it if it is in his possession. ...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah). It states that a person who is coerced by imprisonment to sell or buy has the option (khiyar) to either uphold the transaction or annul it. The ruling distinguishes between the initial contract and later acceptance: if the coerced party voluntarily takes the price after the coercion ends, that act constitutes ratification, and the sale becomes binding. However, if he takes the price under continued coercion, it does not count as ratification, and he must return the price if it is still in his possession. The proof is from Quran 4:29, which requires mutual consent for trade. The author explains that a contract made by a sane adult is valid in form (due to offer and acceptance) but is not binding if made under duress; it becomes non-binding (ghayr lazim). The principle is that contracts requiring consent are not binding if coercion is present. The hadith from Shurayh defines certain acts as coercion: imprisonment, beating, shackling, and threats. The commentary also clarifies that minor threats do not constitute legal coercion; only threats of death, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment count. The sub-section (3) further explains that if the coerced seller voluntarily takes the price, that is a sign of ratification; if he takes it under duress, the sale is annulled and he must return the price if it remains.

Key Terms
اکراه (ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will, defined here as threats of death, severe beating, or prolonged imprisonment.
خيار (khiyar): The option given to the coerced party to either uphold or annul the contract.
حابس (habs): Imprisonment; considered one form of coercion that triggers the option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling on a sale made by a person coerced by imprisonment?
A) The sale is invalid (batil) from the start.
B) The sale is valid and binding if the price is taken voluntarily.
C) The sale is valid but the coerced party can annul it unless he later voluntarily accepts the price.
D) The sale is valid only if the coercion is proven by witnesses.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is valid but non-binding; voluntary acceptance of the price after coercion ends ratifies the contract.

Liability When Coerced Sale Item Is Destroyed
Ruling on liability when coerced sale item is destroyed by willing buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two options for the coerced seller under Hanafi law when the sold item is destroyed by the willing buyer, and explain the legal basis (direct and indirect causation).

Core Idea
In a coerced sale, if the willing buyer destroys the item, the seller can claim from either the buyer or the coercer.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi law of coercion (ikrah). A seller is forced to sell, but the buyer is willing. The item is then destroyed in the buyer's hand. You will learn that the Hanafi school grants the seller two distinct claims based on the principles of direct and indirect causation, supported by Quranic and Prophetic proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize the three parties: the coerced seller (mukrah), the willing buyer (mustari), and the coercer (mukrih). Note that the buyer is not coerced.
The coercion only affects the seller's consent.
2
State the Hanafi Ruling
The seller has two options: claim the value from the buyer because the destruction occurred in his hand, or claim from the coercer because his coercion caused the item to enter the buyer's possession.
The seller may choose whichever is easier to collect.
3
Analyze the First Proof (Quran 4:92)
The verse imposes diyah on the killer, establishing that the one who directly causes destruction (إتلاف مباشر) is liable. By qiyas, the buyer who directly destroys the item is liable.
This is a clear analogy (qiyas jali).
4
Analyze the Second Proof (Hadith on Freeing a Slave)
The Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that a partner who frees a slave must compensate other partners, showing that one who causes a loss indirectly can be held liable.
This hadith supports liability of the coercer as an indirect cause.
5
Apply the Analogy
The coercer's action (forcing the sale) is the cause of the item being in the buyer's hands, leading to its destruction. Thus, the coercer is liable, just like the freeing partner in the hadith.
This is qiyas based on the effective cause (illa) of causation of loss.

Source Text

بائع کو کسی نے مجبور کر کے بیچ کرایا۔ البتہ مشتری کو مجبور نہیں کیا تھا بلکہ اس نے خوشی سے خریدا تھا۔ مشتری کو چاہئے کہ بیع توڑ دے اور طبیع بائع کی طرف واپس کردے۔ لیکن ایسا نہیں کرنے پایا کہ مبیع مشتری کے ہاتھ سے ہلاک ہوگئی ۔ اب بائع کو دو اختیار ہیں ۔ یا مشتری کو بیع کی قیمت کا ضامن بنائے کیونکہ اصیل ہلاک تو اسی کے ہاتھ سے ہوئی ہے۔ اور یہ بھی اختیار ہے کہ جس نے مجبور کیا تھا اس کو ضامن بنائے۔کیونکہ اس کے مجبور کرنے کی وجہ سے مبیع مشتری کے ہاتھ میں گئی ہے۔ اس لئے اس کو بھی ضامن بنا سکتا ہے۔ ضائع کرنے والا ضامن ہو اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ ومن قتل مأمنا خطأ فتحرير رقبة مؤمنة ودية مسلمة الى اهله (الف)
( آیت ۹۲ سورۃ النساء ) اس آیت میں جس نے قتل کیا اس پر دیتے لازم ہوئی جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ضائع کرنے والا ضامن ہوگا۔ اور جو ضائع ہونے کا سبب بنے اس پر بھی ضمان آسکتا ہے اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن عمر ان رسول الله الله قال من اعتق شـركـا لـه في عبد فكان له مال يبلغ ثمن العبد قوم العبد عليه قيمة عدل فاعطى شركائه حصصهم وعتق عليه العبد (ب) ( بخاری شریف، اذا احتق عبدا بین اثنین ادامه بین الشرکا، ص، نمبر ۲۵۲۲) اس حدیث میں شریک کے آزاد کرنے سے دوسرے شریک کو نقصان ہوا تو حضور نے یوں فرمایا کہ دوسرے شریک کے حصے کی قیمت آزاد کرنے والا ادا کرے اور یہ حصہ بھی اسی کی جانب سے آزاد ہو جائے ۔ جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس کے سبب سے نقصان ہوا ہو اس پر ضمان لازم کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ یہاں مجبور کرنے والے کے سبب سے ضائع ہوا ہے اس لئے مجبور کرنے والے سے بھی بائع ضمان لے سکتا ہے۔
(4) And if the sold item perishes in the hand of the buyer while he is not coerced, he is liable for its value, and the coerced person [the seller] may hold the coercer liable if he wishes. [2968] (4) If the sold item perishes in the hand of the buyer while he is not coerced, he is liable for its value. And the coerced person may hold the coercer liable if he wishes. Commentary: The seller was coerced by someone to sell. The buyer was not coerced but bought willingly. The buyer should cancel the sale and return the item to the seller, but it was destroyed. Now the seller has two options: either hold the buyer liable for the value because the destruction occurred in his hand, or hold the coercer liable because it was due to his coercion that the item went into the buyer's hand. Evidence that the destroyer is liable: Quran 4:92, which imposes diyah on the killer, showing that the destroyer is liable. Evidence that the cause of destruction can be liable: Hadith of Ibn Umar (Bukhari) about a partner freeing a slave, where the freeing partner must compensate the other partners for their shares, showing that the one who causes loss can be held liable. Here, the coercer caused the loss, so the seller can claim from him.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah). The passage presents a scenario where a seller is coerced into selling an item, but the buyer is not coerced and purchases willingly. The sold item is then destroyed in the buyer's hand. The Hanafi ruling gives the seller two options: (1) claim the value from the buyer, since he destroyed it, or (2) claim from the coercer, since his coercion caused the item to enter the buyer's possession. The proof is from the Quran (Surah Nisa, 4:92) establishing that the one who causes destruction is liable for compensation, and from a hadith in Sahih Bukhari where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that a partner who frees a slave without permission must compensate the other partners for their shares. This hadith demonstrates that the one who causes loss (even indirectly) can be held liable. The Hanafi school applies this principle here: the coercer, as the cause of the loss, can be made to pay.

Key Terms
مكره (mukrah): The person who was coerced (the seller in this context)
ضمان (daman): Liability for compensation due to destruction or loss

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the buyer
B) Only the coercer
C) Either the buyer or the coercer
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
A coerced seller whose good is destroyed by a willing buyer can claim its value from either the buyer or the coercer, based on direct and indirect causation.

Permission to Destroy Property Under Duress with Liability on Coercer
Ruling with proof from Quran and analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying a Muslim's property under duress of life or limb, identify the liable party (the coercer), and explain the Quranic proof through qiyas.

Core Idea
Under duress of life or limb, destroying another's property is permissible; the coercer bears liability, not the coerced.

This passage addresses a critical question: if someone threatens your life or limb, forcing you to destroy a Muslim's property, may you do so? The Hanafi school rules that you may, and the coercer alone is liable for compensation. The proof is derived from the Quranic principle of necessity and analogical reasoning. Understanding this ruling clarifies who bears responsibility in such emergencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (destroying a Muslim's property) and the legal classification (permissible/mubah) under the condition of duress to life or limb.
The act is destroying property; the ruling is mubah (permissible) due to necessity.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence cited: Quran 2:173 regarding necessity making haram things halal. This is the foundation of the analogy.
The text quotes the verse and states that just as consuming carrion becomes halal under necessity, destroying property becomes halal.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain the logical link (qiyas) between the verse and the ruling. The illa (effective cause) is necessity (darurah).
The analogy extends from consumption of haram to destruction of property because both involve harm prevented by necessity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify that some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold the direct destroyer liable, whereas Hanafi places liability on the coercer.
This ikhtilaf affects who pays compensation; Hanafi position protects the coerced.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Confirm that the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school is that the coercer is liable, and the act is permissible under genuine duress.
The source states the ruled position is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text

[2973] (9) And if he is forced to destroy the property of a Muslim by a threat that he fears for his life or for a limb, it is permissible for him to do that, and the owner of the property has the right to demand compensation from the coercer. [2974] (10) If he is forced to destroy a Muslim's property by such a threat that there is fear for life or for a limb, it is permissible for him to do so. And the owner of the property has the right to take compensation from the coercer. Or this is easier than the first two issues. In this, someone forced another to destroy a Muslim's property with the threat of killing or cutting a limb. In such a situation, it is permissible for him to destroy the property and save his life or limb. Then the owner has two options: either take compensation from the destroyer or from the one who forced him. The permission to destroy property is because at the time of necessity, consuming another's property becomes halal, just as consuming haram things becomes halal. So destroying it will also become halal. (2) The verse says: 'He has only forbidden to you dead animals, blood, the flesh of swine, and that which has been dedicated to other than Allah. But whoever is forced [by necessity], neither desiring [it] nor transgressing [the limit], there is no sin upon him. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:173) (2) In this verse, when a person is forced, the haram thing becomes halal and there is no sin in using it. By analogy, destroying another's property is also made halal. And he can take compensation from the destroyer and also from the coercer; its proof has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on destroying a Muslim's property under duress. The text states that if a person is forced by a credible threat to his life or limb to destroy another Muslim's property, it is permissible for him to do so. The owner can claim compensation from the coercer. The commentary explains that the owner has two options: claim from the destroyer (the one under duress) or from the coercer. The permissibility is based on the principle of necessity, citing the Quranic verse (2:173) regarding consuming carrion under necessity. By qiyas (analogy), destroying property is permissible. The Hanafi position is that the direct destroyer is excused due to duress, but the coercer bears liability. The liability of the coercer is established through previous evidence.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation for property destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The person who destroyed the property.
B) The coercer only.
C) Both the destroyer and coercer are liable jointly.
D) No one is liable.

Key Takeaway
Under duress of life or limb, destroying a Muslim's property is permissible, and the coercer alone must compensate the owner.

Coercion to Destroy Property: Permissibility and Liability
Ruling on coercion to destroy property with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying another's property under coercion and identify the owner's two options for claiming compensation.

Core Idea
Destroying property under coercion to save life is permissible; the owner may claim from either the destroyer or the coercer.

This passage from a classical Hanafi text addresses a critical scenario: a person threatens your life or limb unless you destroy someone else's property. Is it permissible to do so? And who bears liability? The ruling grants permission by analogy to the Quranic permission to consume forbidden food under duress, but crucially, the owner retains the right to claim compensation from either party. Let's walk through the proof and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Note the scenario: coercion with threat to life or limb. The ruling is that it is permissible (ja'iz) for the coerced person to destroy the property to save himself.
The permission is conditional upon the threat being credible and immediate.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The text cites Surah al-Baqarah 2:173, which permits consuming forbidden food under duress. Identify the key phrase 'فَلَا إِثْمَ عَلَيْهِ' (no sin upon him).
This proof is qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalalah in transmission and meaning.
3
Apply qiyas (analogy)
The text analogizes destroying property to consuming forbidden food. Both are normally prohibited but become permissible under necessity. The effective cause (illah) is the state of duress where life/limb is at stake.
Hanafi usul permits qiyas on Quranic permissions for darurah.
4
Determine the liability options
The owner has two options: claim compensation from the direct destroyer (mubashir) or from the coercer (mukrih). The coercer is considered the effective cause (sabab) of the damage.
This is based on earlier proofs not detailed here; the ruling is mufta bihi.

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الرابع ) ۲۲۹ كتاب الاكراه عضو من اعضائه وَسِعَه ان يفعل ذلك ولصاحب المال ان يُضمِّن المكره دی یا عضو کاٹنے کی دھمکی دے کر کی ۔ ایسی صورت میں اس کے لئے گنجائش ہے کہ اس کے مال کو ضائع کر دے اور اپنی جان یا عضو بچالے۔ پھر صاحب مال کو دو اختیار ہیں یا تو ضائع کرنے والے سے ضمان لے لے یا جس نے مجبور کیا ہے اس سے منمان لے لے۔ مال ضائع کرنے کی اجازت تو اس لئے ہو جائے گی کہ مجبوری کے موقع پر دوسرے کا مال کھانا حلال ہو جاتا ہے۔ اسی طرح حرام چیزوں کا کھانا حلال ہو جاتا ہے۔ اس لئے اس کا ضائع کرنا بھی حلال ہو جائے گا (۲) آیت میں ہے۔ انما حرم عليكم الميتة والدم ولحم الـخـنـزيـر ومـا اهل به لغير الله فمن اضطر غير باغ ولا عاد فلا اثم عليه ان الله غفور رحيم (الف) (آیت ۱۷۳ سورة البقرة (۲) اس آیت میں ہے کہ آدمی مجبور ہو جائے تو حرام چیز بھی حلال ہو جاتی ہے اور اس کو استعمال کرنے میں کوئی گناہ نہیں ہوتا۔ اسی پر قیاس کر کے دوسرے کے مال کو بھی ضائع کرنا حلال ہو جائیگا۔ اور ضائع کرنے والے سے ضمان لے سکتا ہے اور مجبور کرنے والے سے بھی ضمان لے سکتا ہے اس کی دلیل پہلے گزر چکی ہے۔
If he is coerced with [threat to] a limb of his limbs, it is permissible for him to do that [destroy property], and the owner of the property has the right to claim compensation from the coercer. Explanation: If he is threatened with cutting a limb or killing, in such a situation there is leeway for him to destroy the property and save his life or limb. Then the owner of the property has two options: either take compensation from the destroyer or take it from the one who coerced him. Permission to destroy property is because in a situation of duress, consuming another's property becomes permissible. Similarly, consuming forbidden things becomes permissible. Therefore, destroying it also becomes permissible. The verse says: 'He has only forbidden to you dead animals, blood, the flesh of swine, and that which has been dedicated to other than Allah. But whoever is forced [by necessity], neither desiring [it] nor transgressing [its limit], there is no sin upon him. Indeed, Allah is Forgiving and Merciful.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:173) This verse indicates that when a person is compelled, forbidden things become permissible and there is no sin. By analogy, destroying another's property becomes permissible. And he can claim compensation from the one who destroyed and from the one who coerced; its proof has passed earlier.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses a situation where a person is coerced (under threat of loss of life or limb) to destroy someone else's property. The ruling states that such destruction is permissible for the coerced person, and the owner of the property has the option to claim compensation (daman) either from the coercer or from the coerced person who destroyed it. The text provides evidence from the Quranic verse on duress (Surah al-Baqarah 2:173), which permits consuming forbidden things under necessity, and then applies qiyas (analogy) to permit destroying another's property to save oneself. The commentary explains that the permission is analogized from the ruling on consuming haram items in dire necessity. The owner's right to choose the party from whom to claim compensation is established by earlier proofs. The passage does not specify the exact ruling for the coercer's liability in detail here, but focuses on the permissibility for the coerced party.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion: compelling someone to do something against their will by threat of harm such as death or loss of limb.
ضمان (daman): Liability or compensation for damage or loss of property.
مكره (mukrih): The person who coerces another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a person is coerced under threat of death to destroy another's property, who can the owner claim compensation from?
A) Only from the person who actually destroyed the property
B) Only from the coercer
C) Either from the destroyer or the coercer
D) From neither because the act was done under duress

Key Takeaway
Under coercion to save life or limb, destroying another's property is permissible, and the owner may seek compensation from either the coerced person or the coercer.

The Prohibition of Selling Weapons to Ahl al-Harb
Ruling prohibiting sale and supply of weapons to enemy combatants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to enemy combatants and identify the key proofs that support this ruling.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to enemies is prohibited because it strengthens them against Muslims.

This lesson examines a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: it is not permissible to sell or send weapons and war materials to those at war with Muslims. The matn uses the phrase 'لا ينبغي' (it is not appropriate), which in Hanafi fiqh signals a strong prohibition. The commentary supports this with three proofs: a hadith from the Prophet ﷺ forbidding the sale of weapons during fitna, an athar from Hasan al-Basri extending the prohibition to horses and anything that aids them, and an incident with Thumama ibn Uthal showing that even food was restricted when it could strengthen the enemy. Understanding this ruling helps Muslims protect the community from harm in commercial transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling or sending weapons and war materials (including mounts and anything that aids them) to ahl al-harb (people of war). The ruling is that it is prohibited – classified as makruh tahrimi in the Hanafi school.
The term 'لا ينبغي' indicates a strong prohibition akin to haram but based on zanni evidence.
2
Locate the primary hadith proof
The matn cites a hadith from Imran ibn Husayn: the Prophet ﷺ forbade selling weapons during fitna. Since ahl al-harb are the ultimate source of fitna, this prohibition applies directly.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so the ruling is not qat'i but still binding in practice.
3
Examine the additional proofs
The commentary adds two more proofs: 1) Hasan al-Basri stated that nothing of weapons, horses, or their aids should be sent to ahl al-harb. 2) The story of Thumama ibn Uthal shows that even grain was withheld from Mecca without the Prophet's permission, illustrating that anything that could strengthen the enemy is included.
These proofs expand the scope beyond weapons to any material that directly or indirectly aids the enemy's war effort.
4
Understand the rationale and scope
The legal basis is preventing harm to Muslims – the enemy should not be given means to fight. The prohibition applies even in peacetime if the enemy is expected to use the supplies against Muslims.
The ruling is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) across schools, though some extend it to other goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينبغي ان يباع السلاح من اهل الحرب ولا يجهز اليهم عن عمران بن حصين قال نهى رسول الله عن بيع السلاح في الفتنة عن الحسن قال لا يبعث الى اهل الحرب شيء من السلاح والكراع ولا ما يستعان على السلاح والكراع سمع ابا هريرة قال ولا والله لا يأتيكم من اليمامة حبة حنطة حتى يأذن فيها النبي الله ولا ما يستعان به على السلاح والكراع
It is not appropriate to sell weapons to the people of war nor to send them [supplies]. From Imran ibn Husayn: the Messenger of Allah forbade selling weapons during fitna. From al-Hasan: nothing of weapons, horses, or anything that aids weapons and horses should be sent to the people of war. He heard Abu Hurayrah say: 'No, by Allah, not a single grain of wheat will come to you from al-Yamamah until the Prophet permits it.' And nothing that is used to aid weapons and horses.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn and its proofs. The ruling states that it is not appropriate to sell or send weapons to the people of war (ahl al-harb). The commentary expands with three proofs: 1) A hadith from Imran ibn Husayn that the Prophet (PBUH) forbade selling weapons during fitna, and ahl al-harb are the greatest source of fitna. 2) An athar from Hasan al-Basri that nothing of weapons, horses, or any aid to them should be sent to ahl al-harb. 3) A hadith from Thumama ibn Uthal where he refused to send wheat to Mecca without the Prophet's permission, indicating that even non-weapon supplies that could strengthen the enemy are restricted. The Hanafi position is that selling or supplying weapons to enemies is prohibited due to the harm it causes to Muslims.

Key Terms
السلاح (al-silah): Weapons and arms used in warfare.
الكراع (al-kura'): Horses and mounts used in battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible as long as there is no war
B) Makruh tanzihi
C) Makruh tahrimi or haram
D) Permissible with a condition of peace

Key Takeaway
Selling weapons to enemy combatants is prohibited (makruh tahrimi) based on the Prophet's prohibition of selling weapons during fitna and the general principle of preventing harm to Muslims.

The Original Owner's Option When a Trader Buys Captured Property
Ruling on option of original owner when a trader purchases his property from the enemy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the original owner's option when a Muslim trader purchases his captured property from a harbi and apply the condition that the owner must pay the exact price paid by the trader.

Core Idea
The original owner must reimburse the trader's cost to reclaim captured property — no free recovery.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a Muslim's property is taken by a harbi (enemy non-Muslim), then a Muslim trader buys it from the harbi and brings it to Dar al-Islam. The original owner cannot simply take it back for free; he has an option (khiyar) to reclaim it by paying the trader the exact purchase price. This balances the trader's right to avoid loss with the owner's right to recover his property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the facts: a Muslim's property was captured by a harbi; a Muslim trader later buys that property from the harbi and brings it into Dar al-Islam.
The trader acts voluntarily, not under duress.
2
State the ruling
The original owner has a choice (khiyar): he may reclaim the property by paying the trader the exact price the trader paid (thaman), or he may leave it and not reclaim.
The owner is not obliged to reclaim; he can choose either option.
3
Determine the hukm level
The owner's option is mubah (permissible) — he is not commanded or prohibited. The trader's ownership is valid, but the owner is given a right to buy it back at cost.
This is a specific ruling unique to this context.
4
Identify the dalil
The hadith narrated by Ibn Abbas (Dar Qutni): 'fa in shaa akhadhahu bi al-thaman' — If he wishes, he takes it with the price. The phrase 'bi al-thaman' specifically indicates the price paid by the trader.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted by Hanafi jurists.
5
Explain the wisdom
The ruling prevents harm to the trader (who invested money) while allowing the owner to recover what was wrongfully taken. This aligns with the principle 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm).
Without this option, the trader would suffer loss, which is prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۳۰۲۱ (۴۲) وَإِنْ دَخَلَ دَارَ الْحَرْبِ تَاجِرٌ فَاشْتَرَى ذَلِكَ فَأَخْرَجَهُ إِلَى دَارِ الْإِسْلَامِ فَمَالِكُهُ الْأَوَّلُ بِالْخِيَارِ إِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَهُ بِالثَّمَنِ الَّذِي اشْتَرَاهُ بِهِ التَّاجِرُ وَإِنْ شَاءَ تَرَكَهُ.
اگر ہمارا تا جر دار الحرب میں داخل ہو اور اس کو خرید کر دار الاسلام لائے تو اس کے پہلے مالک کو اختیار ہے چاہے تو اتنی قیمت سے جتنے میں تاجر نے خریدا ہے لے اور چاہے تو چھوڑ دے۔
کسی مسلمان کا مال حربی لے گیا تھا، ہمارے مسلمان تاجر نے اس سے خرید کر دار الاسلام لایا تو مالک چاہے تو جتنی قیمت دے کر تاجر لایا ہے اتنی قیمت تاجر کو دے کر اپنا مال لے لے اور قیمت نہ دینا چاہے تو چھوڑ دے۔ تاجر نے رقم دی ہے اس لئے اس سے مفت لینے میں اس کا گھاٹا ہے جو لا ضرر ولا ضرار حدیث کے خلاف ہے۔ البتہ جتنی قیمت دی ہے اتنی قیمت دے کر اس کا بھی نقصان نہیں ہے اور مالک کا بھی فائدہ ہے۔ اس لئے قیمت دے کر مال لے ورنہ چھوڑ دے (۲) اوپر کی حدیث میں تھا۔ فَإِنْ شَاءَ أَخَذَهُ بِالثَّمَنِ (دار قطنی، کتاب السیر، ج رابع، ص ۶۴ نمبر ۴۱۵۵) اس میں بَالثَّمَنِ سے اشارہ ہے کہ جتنی قیمت دی ہے وہ ادا کرے۔ کیونکہ ثمن کہتے ہیں پہلی دی ہوئی قیمت کو، جس سے اشارہ ہوتا ہے کہ پہلے جو قیمت دی ہے اتنی ہی دے کر واپس لے۔
If a trader enters Dar al-Harb and purchases that, then brings it out to Dar al-Islam, then its original owner has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it with the price by which the trader purchased it, and if he wishes, he may leave it. [Urdu: If a Muslim trader enters Dar al-Harb and buys it and brings it to Dar al-Islam, the original owner has the option: if he wants, he gives the price the trader paid and takes his property; if he does not want to give the price, he may leave it. The trader paid money, so taking it free would cause him loss, contrary to the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar'.] The hadith: 'if he wishes, he takes it with the price (al-thaman)' indicates he pays exactly what the trader paid.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh on a specific scenario: a Muslim trader enters Dar al-Harb and buys property that had been captured by harbis from a Muslim. When the trader brings it to Dar al-Islam, the original owner has an option (khiyar). He may reclaim his property by paying the trader the exact purchase price (thaman) — not the market value. This prevents loss to the trader (who invested money) while allowing the original owner to recover what was wrongfully taken. The principle is based on the hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm). The hadith of Ibn Abbas with the expression 'bi al-thaman' supports that the price paid is the basis.

Key Terms
الثمن (al-thaman): The purchase price paid by the trader when he bought the property from the harbi.
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option given to the original owner to either take the property by paying the price or leave it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Nothing
D) Double the price

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling gives the original owner of captured property a choice to reclaim it by paying the exact amount the trader paid, balancing ownership rights with the principle of no harm.

The Owner's Option to Reclaim Goods Purchased from Dar al-Harb
Ruling on a merchant buying goods in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a Muslim merchant buys captured goods from enemy territory and bring them to Dar al-Islam, and identify the owner's option based on the purchase price.

Core Idea
The original owner may reclaim his goods by paying the exact purchase price, not the market value.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in the context of war and trade: a Muslim merchant purchases goods that were originally taken by the enemy, and then brings them into Muslim lands. The original owner is given a legal option (khiyar) to reclaim the property, but not for free—he must compensate the merchant for what he paid. This rule balances the merchant's right to recover his expenditure with the owner's right to regain his property, grounded in the principle of no harm (la darar).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, recognize the act: a merchant enters Dar al-Harb (enemy territory), buys goods there, and brings them to Dar al-Islam. The ruling: the purchase is valid, but the original owner has an option.
The option is a khiyar (right to rescind) for the owner.
2
Define the Owner's Option
The owner may either reclaim the goods by paying the merchant the exact purchase price (thaman) or abandon the claim. He cannot demand the goods for free or at a different price.
The term thaman specifically refers to the price the merchant paid, not market value.
3
Locate the Dalil
The ruling is supported by a hadith from Dar Qutni: 'if he wishes, he may take it for the price.' The phrase 'bil-thaman' (with the price) indicates the purchase price must be paid.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing the rule in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Understand the Wisdom Behind the Rule
The rule prevents loss to the merchant (who paid for the goods) and respects the owner's right, consistent with the maxim 'la darar wa la dirar' (no harm and no reciprocating harm).
If the owner could reclaim for free, the merchant would suffer unjust loss.

If a merchant enters Dar al-Harb and buys something and brings it out to Dar al-Islam, its first owner has the option: if he wishes he may take it for the price the merchant bought it for, and if he wishes he may leave it.

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on goods purchased from enemy territory by a Muslim merchant. The original owner (likely a Muslim whose property was taken by the enemy) has the option to reclaim the item by paying the merchant the exact purchase price, or to abandon it. The Arabic term 'thaman' (price) is interpreted strictly as the amount the merchant paid, not the market value. The commentary cites a hadith from Dar Qutni to support that the owner may take it 'with the price'.

Prohibition of Treachery for Muslim Trader in Dar al-Harb
Ruling on a Muslim entering as a trader and betraying the enemy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a Muslim trader in Dar al-Harb who commits treachery, explain why the property becomes milk mahzur, and apply the two hadith proofs to justify the requirement of sadaqah.

Core Idea
Entering as a trader implies aman; betraying it makes the property milk mahzur, requiring sadaqah.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in Hanafi siyar: a Muslim enters enemy territory (Dar al-Harb) as a trader, which by implication grants him an aman (safe conduct) — he is trusted not to harm the enemy. The ruling prohibits treachery (ghadr) against that trust. If he violates it and takes property, he technically becomes owner, but the ownership is classified as milk mahzur (prohibited ownership): he cannot benefit from it and must give it in charity. The text provides two hadith proofs: one from Buraidah prohibiting ghulul and ghadr in general, and another from al-Miswar where the Prophet rejects property acquired through treachery by al-Mughirah. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the opening sentence: a Muslim entering Dar al-Harb as a trader is prohibited from touching enemy property or lives. If he betrays and takes property, he owns it with milk mahzur.
Note the implicit aman.
2
Understand the basis of implicit aman
The explanation (sharh) clarifies that entering as a trader implies a covenant not to violate trust. Therefore, ghadr is forbidden.
Distinguish from a warrior without aman.
3
Apply the first hadith proof (Buraidah)
The hadith commands: 'Do not be treacherous, do not betray.' This directly prohibits ghadr in any context, including this scenario.
This establishes the prohibition.
4
Apply the second hadith proof (al-Miswar)
When al-Mughirah brought property from treachery, the Prophet refused it, saying it is 'property of treachery.' This shows that such property cannot be kept; it must be disposed of in charity.
This establishes the remedy: sadaqah.
5
Note the Hanafi distinction and consequence
The property is not considered spoils (ghanimah) requiring khums; it is milk mahzur. The owner must give it in charity, not keep it or return it.
Cross-madhhab: some may treat it as booty.

Source Text

وان لم يأذن لهم الامام [۳۰۴۹ (۷۰) واذا دخل المسلم دار الحرب تاجرا فلا يحل له ان يتعرض لشيء من اموالهم ولا من دمائهم فان غدر بهم ف فأخذ شيئا ملكه ملكا محظورا

حاشیہ : (الف) جس کو خاص طور پر بھیجتے تو عام لشکر کے حصے کے علاوہ اس کو نفل دیتے تا ہم ان تمام میں شمس واجب ہوتا (ب ) آپ جب کسی لشکر پر امیر بناتے تو فرماتے جس نے اللہ کے ساتھ کفر کیا ہے اس سے جنگ کرو ۔ لیکن خیانت نہ کرو، غدر نہ کرو اور مسئلہ نہ کرو، اور بچے کوقتل نہ کرو (ج) حضور صلح حدیبیہ کے سال دس سو سے زیادہ صحابہ کے ساتھ نکلے حضرت مغیرہ زمانہ جاہلیت میں کچھ لوگوں کے ساتھ رہے تھے اور ان کو قتل کر کے مال لیا تھا ، پھر آ کر مسلمان ہوئے تھے تو آپ نے فرمایا بہر حال اسلام تو تمہارا قبول کرتا ہوں، بہر حال مال تو دھو کے کا مال ہے، مجھے اس کی ضرورت نہیں ہے۔
And if the Imam did not permit them [3049 (70)] And if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb as a trader, it is not permissible for him to touch any of their property or their blood. So if he betrays them and takes something, he owns it with a prohibited ownership.
Principle: This issue is based on the principle that if one acts understanding the Imam's intent, khums will be taken and that action will be permissible.
And if the Imam himself sends a detachment, then there is khums in its spoils. There is evidence for this in this hadith: From Abdullah bin Umar that the Messenger of Allah would give nafal (extra share) to some of those he sent from the detachments for themselves specifically, apart from the general army's division, and khums was wajib in all of that. (a) (Abu Dawud, Book of Nafal for the Detachment Leaving the Army, vol.2, p.20, no.2746) In this hadith, khums used to be obligatory in the entire spoils of every detachment.
Milah: derived from man'a, meaning power to prevent. (3049) (70) If a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb as a trader, it is not permissible for him to touch property or life. So if he betrays them and takes something, he becomes the owner of it in a prohibited manner, and he will be ordered to give it in charity.
Explanation: When he goes as a trader, it is as if he carries an aman (safe conduct) that he will not violate the covenant. Therefore he should not commit treachery or deception, nor should he harm the lives or property of the harbis. And if he does commit treachery and brings their property to Dar al-Islam, he becomes its owner. But since he became owner through treachery, the ownership is prohibited (milk mahzur) and he will be ordered to give that property in charity.
The evidence for not committing treachery is this hadith: From Sulaiman bin Buraidah from his father, he said: When the Messenger of Allah appointed an amir over an army... he would say: 'Fight those who disbelieve in Allah. Launch raids, but do not be treacherous, do not betray, do not mutilate, and do not kill children.' (b) (Muslim, Book of the Imam Appointing Commanders over Armies..., vol.2, p.82, no.1731; Abu Dawud, Book of Inviting the Polytheists..., p.1361, no.2613) From this hadith it is understood that even in Dar al-Harb, treachery and deception should not be committed. And if one takes the property of harbis by treachery, the ownership is prohibited. The evidence for this is the hadith: From al-Miswar bin Makhramah, he said: The Messenger of Allah set out during the year of Hudaybiyyah with over a thousand of his Companions... And al-Mughirah had accompanied some people in the Jahiliyyah, killed them and taken their property, then came and accepted Islam. The Prophet said: 'As for Islam, we have accepted it. As for the property, it is property of treachery; we have no need for it.' (c) (Abu Dawud, Book of Peace with the Enemy, vol.2, p.124, no.2765 / Bukhari, Book of Conditions in Jihad and Peace Treaties..., p.1377, no.2731) This
Footnote: (a) He used to give extra shares (nafal) to those he sent specifically, apart from the general army's share, yet in all of that khums was obligatory. (b) When he appointed an amir over an army, he would say: 'Fight those who disbelieve in Allah. But do not be treacherous, do not betray, do not mutilate, and do not kill children.' (c) The Prophet set out in the year of Hudaybiyyah with over a thousand companions. Al-Mughirah had accompanied some people in the Jahiliyyah, killed them and taken their property, then came and accepted Islam. The Prophet said: 'As for Islam, we have accepted it from you. As for the property, it is property of treachery; I have no need for it.'
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific scenario: a Muslim who enters Dar al-Harb (enemy territory) under the guise of a trader, implying he has an implicit aman (safe conduct) to conduct trade without harming the enemy. The ruling is that he is prohibited from betraying that trust by taking enemy property or killing. If he does commit treachery and takes property, he technically becomes the owner of that property (i.e., it is not considered stolen in the sense that he must return it), but the ownership is 'milk mahzur' (prohibited ownership) – meaning he is forbidden to benefit from it, and he must give it in charity (sadaqah). This is based on the principle that treachery is forbidden (as per the hadith of Buraidah) and that ill-gotten gains from treachery cannot be retained (as per the hadith of al-Miswar regarding al-Mughirah). The Hanafi position distinguishes this from a warrior entering without permission: the trader is under a form of covenant, so his act is treachery, while a warrior acting on implied intent is not. The authority weight is from Zahir al-Riwayah, as the proofs are from established hadith. The footnote reiterates the prohibition of treachery and clarifies that even when nafal (extra shares) were given, khums was still wajib on all spoils.

Key Terms
ملک محظور (milk mahzur): Prohibited ownership: the person legally owns the item but is forbidden to use it and must give it in charity.
غدر (ghadr): Breach of trust or covenant; treachery, specifically violating the implied aman given to a trader.
امان (aman): Safe conduct or promise of security; implicit in the case of a trader entering Dar al-Harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a Muslim do with property taken from harbis by treachery after entering Dar al-Harb as a trader?
A) Keep it as booty and pay khums.
B) Return it to the harbis.
C) Give it in charity (sadaqah).
D) Use it for personal benefit.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim trader in Dar al-Harb is under an implied aman; betraying it makes the property milk mahzur, which must be given in charity.

Assessing Kharaj According to Land Capacity
Ruling on adjusting kharaj according to land capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adjusting kharaj based on land capacity and explain its proof from the practice of Caliph Umar.

Core Idea
Kharaj must be set within the land's capacity; the Imam may reduce it if the land cannot bear the tax.

This passage from Kitab al-Siyar addresses the assessment of kharaj, the land tax imposed on conquered agricultural lands. The key principle is that the tax should be proportionate to the land's productive capacity. The Imam has the authority to reduce the tax if the land cannot bear the pre-set amount. The commentary provides evidence from the practice of Caliph Umar, who carefully ensured that the tax was not beyond the people's ability to pay.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the matn carefully. The ruling states that for lands other than those with fixed rates, the tax is imposed according to capacity. If the land cannot bear the imposed amount, the Imam must reduce it.
This applies to conquered lands not covered by an explicit treaty.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence comes from the practice of Caliph Umar. The athar (narration) shows that Umar asked his officials whether the tax was excessive, and instructed them to ensure it was not beyond the land's capacity.
The narration is found in Sahih al-Bukhari and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal implication (wajh) is that Umar's concern demonstrates the principle of capacity. The Imam must not impose a tax that the land cannot bear, and adjustment is permissible.
This is a case of amal al-sahabi (practice of the Companion), which is a source of law in the Hanafi school.
4
Note the practical consequence
The ruling means that kharaj is not a fixed, unchangeable tax. The Imam has discretion to lower it based on actual productivity, preventing hardship.
This flexibility is a mercy in the law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[13063 (84)] وما سوى ذلك من الاصناف يوضع عليها بحسب الطاقة فان لم تُطق ما وضع عليها نقصها الامام
اس کے علاوہ اور قسم کی زمینوں میں طاقت کے مطابق ، اور اگر جو اس پر مقرر کیا ہے اس کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہوتو امام اس سے کم کر دے۔۔ شرق او پر جو متعین کردہ مقدار زمین کی عام پیداوار کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ اس لئے اگر اس سے کم پیداوار ہو تو کم خراج متعین کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ اور جو متعین کیا ہے اگر رعیت اس کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو تو امام اس سے کم بھی کر سکتا ہے۔ حضرت عمرؓ نے خراج متعین کرنے کے بعد حضرت حذیفہ بن الیمان اور عثمان بن حنیف سے دریافت کیا کہ یہ خراج کہیں زیادہ تو نہیں ہے؟ اس پر حضرت عثمان بن حنیف نے فرمایا کہ اس سے زیادہ بھی رکھیں تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ لمبی حدیث کا ٹکڑا یہ ہے۔ رایت عمر بن الخطاب قبل ان يصاب بايام المدينة ووقف على حذيفة بن اليمان وعثمان بن حنيف قال كيف فعلتما حملناها امرا هي له مطيقة ما فيها كبير فضل قال انظرا ان تكونا حملتما الارض مالا تطيق قال قالا لا (الف) ( بخاری شریف، باب قصته البيعة والاتفاق على عثمان بن عفان ص ۵۲۳، نمبر ۳۷۰۰ / مصنف ابن ابی شیته ۲۷، ما قالوافی الخمس والخراج کیف یوضع ، ج سادس ص ۴۳۹، نمبر ۳۲۷۰۸) اس اثر میں ہے کہ اگر زیادہ ہو گیا ہو تو دیکھ لو۔ جس کا مطلب یہ ہوا کہ اس مقدار سے کم بھی کیا جا سکتا ہے۔ اور زمین کی پیداوار کے مطابق خراج لازم کیا جائے گا، مصنف ابن ابی شیبہ کے اثر میں حضرت حذیفہ کا یہ جملہ بھی ہے الو شئت لا ضعفت ارضی جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ اگر چاہیں تو اس سے زیادہ بھی خراج متعین کر سکتے ہیں۔ لیکن حضرت عمرؓ نے اس سے زیادہ متعین نہیں فرمایا۔ اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ طاقت سے زیادہ ہو جائے تو کم بھی کیا جاسکتا ہے (۲) دوسرے اثر میں ہے۔ فوضع عثمـان عـلـى الجريب من الكرم عشرة دراهم وعلى جريب النخل ثمانية دراهم وعلى جريب القصب ستة دراهم يعنى الرطبة وعلى جريب البر اربعة دراهم وعلى جريب الشعير درهمين (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، ۲۷ ما قالوافی احمس الخراج كیف یوضع ، ج سادس ص ۲۳۹، نمبر ۳۲۷۰۴ رسن للمبتی ، باب قدر الخراج الذي وضع على السواد، ج تاسع ہمیں ۲۳۰، نمبر ۱۸۳۸۲) اس اثر میں مختلف پیداوار کا خراج مختلف ہے جس سے معلوم ہوا کہ خراج کم بیش کر سکتا ہے۔
And other than that of the types, it is imposed upon them according to their capacity; if they cannot bear what is imposed, the Imam reduces it.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kitab al-Siyar on the assessment of kharaj (land tax). The ruling states that for lands other than those with fixed kharaj (like the Sawad), the tax is to be set according to the land's productive capacity. If the land cannot bear the imposed amount due to low yield, the Imam is authorized to reduce it. The commentary provides evidence from the practice of Caliph Umar, who, after appointing Hudhayfah and Uthman ibn Hunayf to assess the Kharaj of the Sawad, asked them if the tax was excessive. They replied that the land could bear the amount, but Umar instructed them to ensure it was not beyond the people's capacity. This indicates that the Imam should not impose more than the land can bear, and adjustment is permitted. Additionally, an athar from Uthman ibn Hunayf shows different tax rates for different crops (grapes, dates, vegetables, wheat, barley), proving that the tax varies according to productivity.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): A land tax imposed on conquered agricultural lands, assessed annually based on productivity.
الطاقة (al-taqah): The capacity of the land to produce, meaning the tax should be set according to what the land can bear without undue hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should the Imam do if the land cannot bear the fixed kharaj amount?
A) Increase the kharaj to compensate for low output.
B) Reduce the kharaj according to the land's capacity.
C) Exempt the land entirely from kharaj.
D) Force the owner to cultivate more efficiently.

Key Takeaway
Kharaj is not fixed; it is adjusted by the Imam according to the land's capacity, reflecting the Islamic principle of justice in taxation.
